</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">Alma mater</a></th><td><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Balliol_College,_Oxford" title="Balliol College, Oxford">Balliol College, Oxford</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Inns_of_Court_School_of_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Inns of Court School of Law">Inns of Court School of Law</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Profession</th><td><a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">Barrister</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Signature</th><td><a href="/wiki/File:HH_Asquith_Signature.svg" class="image" title="H. H. Asquith&#39;s signature"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/HH_Asquith_Signature.svg/128px-HH_Asquith_Signature.svg.png" decoding="async" width="128" height="54" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/HH_Asquith_Signature.svg/192px-HH_Asquith_Signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/HH_Asquith_Signature.svg/256px-HH_Asquith_Signature.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="536" data-file-height="226" /></a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:264px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith,_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith,_KG,_PC,_KC,_FRS.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith%2C_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith%2C_KG%2C_PC%2C_KC%2C_FRS.png/262px-Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith%2C_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith%2C_KG%2C_PC%2C_KC%2C_FRS.png" decoding="async" width="262" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith%2C_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith%2C_KG%2C_PC%2C_KC%2C_FRS.png/393px-Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith%2C_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith%2C_KG%2C_PC%2C_KC%2C_FRS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith%2C_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith%2C_KG%2C_PC%2C_KC%2C_FRS.png/524px-Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith%2C_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith%2C_KG%2C_PC%2C_KC%2C_FRS.png 2x" data-file-width="1158" data-file-height="1158" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shield_of_Arms_of_H._H._Asquith,_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith,_KG,_PC,_KC,_FRS.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Garter-encircled shield of arms of H. H. Asquith, 1st Earl of Oxford and Asquith, KG, as displayed on his Order of the Garter stall plate in St. George's Chapel, viz. Sable on a fesse between three cross-crosslets argent, a portcullis of the field.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Herbert Henry Asquith, 1st Earl of Oxford and Asquith</b>, <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:100%;"><a href="/wiki/Knight_of_the_Order_of_the_Garter" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight of the Order of the Garter">KG</a><span style="font-size:14px;">&#44;</span>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">PC</a><span style="font-size:14px;">&#44;</span>&#32;<a href="/wiki/King%27s_Counsel" class="mw-redirect" title="King&#39;s Counsel">KC</a><span style="font-size:14px;">&#44;</span>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (12 September 1852&#160;– 15 February 1928), generally known as <b>H. H. Asquith</b>, was a British statesman and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> politician who served as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> from 1908 to 1916. He was the last prime minister to lead a majority Liberal government, and he played a central role in the design and passage of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_welfare_reforms" title="Liberal welfare reforms">major liberal legislation</a> and a reduction of the power of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>. In August 1914, Asquith took <a href="/wiki/British_entry_into_World_War_I" title="British entry into World War I">Great Britain and the British Empire into the First World War</a>. In 1915, his government was vigorously attacked for a shortage of munitions and the failure of the <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli Campaign</a>. He formed a <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition government</a> with other parties, but failed to satisfy critics. As a result, he was forced to resign in December 1916, and he never regained power.
</p><p>After attending <a href="/wiki/Balliol_College,_Oxford" title="Balliol College, Oxford">Balliol College, Oxford</a>, he became a successful <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barrister</a>. In 1886 he was the Liberal candidate for <a href="/wiki/East_Fife_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Fife (UK Parliament constituency)">East Fife</a>, a seat he held for over thirty years. In 1892 he was appointed as <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> in <a href="/wiki/Liberal_government,_1892%E2%80%931895" title="Liberal government, 1892–1895">Gladstone's fourth ministry</a>, remaining in the post until the Liberals lost the <a href="/wiki/1895_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1895 United Kingdom general election">1895 election</a>. In the decade of opposition that followed Asquith became a major figure in the party, and when the Liberals regained power under <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a> in 1905 Asquith was named <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>. In 1908 Asquith succeeded him as Prime Minister. The Liberals were determined to advance their reform agenda. An impediment to this was the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, which rejected the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People's Budget</a> of 1909. Meanwhile the <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_Act_1909" title="South Africa Act 1909">South Africa Act 1909</a> passed. Asquith called an <a href="/wiki/January_1910_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="January 1910 United Kingdom general election">election for January 1910</a>, and the Liberals won, though were reduced to a <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a>. After another <a href="/wiki/December_1910_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="December 1910 United Kingdom general election">general election in December 1910</a> he gained passage of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a>, allowing a bill three times passed by the Commons in consecutive sessions to be enacted regardless of the Lords. Asquith was less successful in dealing with <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a>. Repeated crises led to <a href="/wiki/Larne_gun-running" title="Larne gun-running">gun running</a> and violence, verging on civil war.
</p><p>When Britain declared war on Germany in response to the German invasion of Belgium, high profile conflicts were suspended regarding Ireland and women's suffrage. Although more of a committee chair than a dynamic leader, he oversaw national mobilisation; the dispatch of the <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_I)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War I)">British Expeditionary Force</a> to the Western Front, the creation of a mass army, and the development of an industrial strategy designed to support the country's war aims. The war became bogged down and the demand rose for better leadership. He was forced to form a <a href="/wiki/Asquith_coalition_ministry" title="Asquith coalition ministry">coalition with the Conservatives and Labour early in 1915</a>. He was weakened by his own indecision over strategy, conscription, and financing.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Lloyd George replaced him as Prime Minister in December 1916. They became bitter enemies and fought for control of the fast-declining Liberal Party. His role in creating the modern <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Welfare state in the United Kingdom">British welfare state</a> (1906–1911) has been celebrated, but his weaknesses as a war leader and as a party leader after 1914 have been highlighted by historians.
</p>

<h2><span id="Early_life_and_career:_1852.E2.80.931908"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career:_1852–1908">Early life and career: 1852–1908</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and career: 1852–1908">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_background">Family background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Family background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg/220px-H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg/330px-H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg/440px-H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="772" data-file-height="902" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:H-H-Asquith-as-a-boy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith (left) with his sister Emily and elder brother William, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1857</span></div></div></div>
<p>Asquith was born in <a href="/wiki/Morley,_West_Yorkshire" title="Morley, West Yorkshire">Morley</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/West_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="West Riding of Yorkshire">West Riding of Yorkshire</a>, the younger son of Joseph Dixon Asquith (1825–1860) and his wife Emily, <i>née</i> Willans (1828–1888). The couple also had three daughters, of whom only one survived infancy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins13_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins13-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> The Asquiths were an old Yorkshire family, with a long <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconformist</a> tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> It was a matter of family pride, shared by Asquith, that an ancestor, Joseph Asquith, was imprisoned for his part in the pro-<a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Roundhead</a> <a href="/wiki/Farnley_Wood_Plot" title="Farnley Wood Plot">Farnley Wood Plot</a> of 1664.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson1-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both Asquith's parents came from families associated with the Yorkshire wool trade. Dixon Asquith inherited the Gillroyd Mill Company, founded by his father. Emily's father, William Willans, ran a successful <a href="/wiki/Wool-stapler" title="Wool-stapler">wool-trading</a> business in <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield" title="Huddersfield">Huddersfield</a>. Both families were middle-class, <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalist</a>, and politically <a href="/wiki/Radicalism_(historical)" title="Radicalism (historical)">radical</a>. Dixon was a mild man, cultivated and in his son's words "not cut out" for a business career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins13_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins13-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> He was described as "a man of high character who held Bible classes for young men".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962194–195_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962194–195-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Emily suffered persistent poor health, but was of strong character, and a formative influence on her sons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962195_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962195-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_schooling">Childhood and schooling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Childhood and schooling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his younger days he was called Herbert ("Bertie" as a child) within the family, but his second wife called him Henry. His biographer <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Koss" title="Stephen Koss">Stephen Koss</a> entitled the first chapter of his biography "From Herbert to Henry", referring to upward social mobility and his abandonment of his Yorkshire Nonconformist roots with his second marriage. However, in public, he was invariably referred to only as <b>H. H. Asquith</b>. "There have been few major national figures whose Christian names were less well known to the public" according to biographer <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins13_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins13-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> He and his brother were educated at home by their parents until 1860, when Dixon Asquith died suddenly. William Willans took charge of the family, moved them to a house near his own, and arranged for the boys' schooling.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins15_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins15-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> After a year at <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_New_College" title="Huddersfield New College">Huddersfield College</a> they were sent as <a href="/wiki/Boarding_school" title="Boarding school">boarders</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fulneck_School" title="Fulneck School">Fulneck School</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian Church</a> school near <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>. In 1863 William Willans died, and the family came under the care of Emily's brother, John Willans. The boys went to live with him in London; when he moved back to Yorkshire in 1864 for business reasons, they remained in London and were lodged with various families. The biographer Naomi Levine writes that in effect Asquith was "treated like an orphan" for the rest of his childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine75_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine75-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The departure of his uncle effectively severed Asquith's ties with his native Yorkshire, and he described himself thereafter as "to all intents and purposes a Londoner".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBates10_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBates10-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Another biographer, <a href="/wiki/H._C._G._Matthew" class="mw-redirect" title="H. C. G. Matthew">H. C. G. Matthew</a>, writes that Asquith's northern nonconformist background continued to influence him: "It gave him a point of sturdy anti-establishmentarian reference, important to a man whose life in other respects was a long absorption into metropolitanism."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The boys were sent to the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London_School" title="City of London School">City of London School</a> as dayboys. Under the school's headmaster, the Rev <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Abbott_Abbott" title="Edwin Abbott Abbott">E. A. Abbott</a>, a distinguished classical scholar, Asquith became an outstanding pupil. He later said that he was under deeper obligations to his old headmaster than to any man living;<sup id="cite_ref-dinner_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dinner-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Abbott disclaimed credit for the boy's progress: "I never had a pupil who owed less to me and more to his own natural ability."<sup id="cite_ref-dinner_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dinner-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson10_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson10-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Asquith excelled in <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a> and English, was little interested in sports, read voraciously in the <a href="/wiki/Guildhall_Library" title="Guildhall Library">Guildhall Library</a>, and became fascinated with oratory. He visited the public gallery of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>, studied the techniques of famous preachers, and honed his own skills in the school debating society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBates10–11_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBates10–11-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Abbott remarked on the cogency and clarity of his pupil's speeches, qualities for which Asquith became celebrated throughout the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson3_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson3-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins17_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins17-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Asquith later recalled seeing, as a schoolboy, the corpses of five murderers left hanging outside <a href="/wiki/Newgate_Prison#Executions" title="Newgate Prison">Newgate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith30_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith30-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxford">Oxford</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Oxford">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In November 1869 Asquith won a <a href="/wiki/Classical_scholarship" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical scholarship">classical scholarship</a> at <a href="/wiki/Balliol_College,_Oxford" title="Balliol College, Oxford">Balliol College, Oxford</a>, going up the following October. The college's prestige, already high, continued to rise under the recently elected Master, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Jowett" title="Benjamin Jowett">Benjamin Jowett</a>. He sought to raise the standards of the college to the extent that its undergraduates shared what Asquith later called a "tranquil consciousness of effortless superiority".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Although Asquith admired Jowett, he was more influenced by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hill_Green" title="Thomas Hill Green">T. H. Green</a>, <a href="/wiki/White%27s_Professor_of_Moral_Philosophy" title="White&#39;s Professor of Moral Philosophy">White's Professor of Moral Philosophy</a>. The abstract side of philosophy did not greatly attract Asquith, whose outlook was always practical, but Green's progressive liberal political views appealed to him.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith-scholarship-announcement.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/af/Asquith-scholarship-announcement.jpg/220px-Asquith-scholarship-announcement.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="85" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/af/Asquith-scholarship-announcement.jpg/330px-Asquith-scholarship-announcement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/af/Asquith-scholarship-announcement.jpg 2x" data-file-width="380" data-file-height="147" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith-scholarship-announcement.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early press mention of Asquith, 1869</div></div></div>
<p>Asquith's university career was distinguished—"striking without being sensational" in the words of his biographer, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a>. An easy grasp of his studies left him ample time to indulge his liking for debate. In the first month at university he spoke at the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Union" title="Oxford Union">Oxford Union</a>. His official biographers, <a href="/wiki/J._A._Spender" title="J. A. Spender">J. A. Spender</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Asquith" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyril Asquith">Cyril Asquith</a>, commented that in his first months at Oxford "he voiced the orthodox Liberal view, speaking in support, <i>inter alia</i>, of the <a href="/wiki/Disestablishment" class="mw-redirect" title="Disestablishment">disestablishment</a> of the Church of England, and of non-intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He sometimes debated against his Balliol contemporary <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner,_1st_Viscount_Milner" title="Alfred Milner, 1st Viscount Milner">Alfred Milner</a>, who although then a Liberal was already an advocate of British imperialism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith31–32_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith31–32-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He was elected Treasurer of the Union in 1872 but was defeated at his first attempt at the Presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith33_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith33-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/1874_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1874 United Kingdom general election">General Election in January and February 1874</a> he spoke against <a href="/wiki/Lord_Randolph_Churchill" title="Lord Randolph Churchill">Lord Randolph Churchill</a>, who was not yet a prominent politician, at nearby <a href="/wiki/Woodstock_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Woodstock (UK Parliament constituency)">Woodstock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith34_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith34-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> He eventually became <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_Oxford_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the Oxford Union">President of the Union</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Term" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity Term">Trinity Term</a> 1874, his last term as an undergraduate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith33–34_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith33–34-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins24_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins24-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith was <i>proxime accessit</i> (runner-up) for the Hertford Prize in 1872, again <i>proxime accessit</i> for the Ireland Prize in 1873, and again for the Ireland in 1874, on that occasion coming so close that the examiners awarded him a special prize of books. However, he won the Craven Scholarship and graduated with what his biographers describe as an "easy" <a href="/wiki/Double_first" class="mw-redirect" title="Double first">double first</a> in <a href="/wiki/Literae_Humaniores" class="mw-redirect" title="Literae Humaniores">Mods and Greats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith32_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith32-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> After graduating he was elected to a prize <a href="/wiki/Fellow" title="Fellow">fellowship</a> of Balliol.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins23_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins23-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_professional_career">Early professional career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early professional career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%;&#32;; background-color: #c6dbf7;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style="">Perhaps because of his stark beginnings, Asquith was always attracted to the comforts and accoutrements that money can buy. He was personally extravagant, always enjoying the good life—good food, good companions, good conversation and attractive women.</div>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">Naomi Levine, in a 1991 biography<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevine76_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevine76-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>After his graduation in 1874, Asquith spent several months coaching <a href="/wiki/Newton_Wallop,_6th_Earl_of_Portsmouth" title="Newton Wallop, 6th Earl of Portsmouth">Viscount Lymington</a>, the 18-year-old son and heir of the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Portsmouth" title="Earl of Portsmouth">Earl of Portsmouth</a>. He found the experience of aristocratic country-house life agreeable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBates12_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBates12-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins25_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins25-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> He liked less the austere side of the nonconformist Liberal tradition, with its strong <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance</a> movement. He was proud of ridding himself of "the Puritanism in which I was bred".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERintala111_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERintala111-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> His fondness for fine wines and spirits, which began at this period, eventually earned him the sobriquet "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/squiffy" class="extiw" title="wikt:squiffy">Squiffy</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERintala118_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERintala118-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Returning to Oxford, Asquith spent the first year of his seven-year fellowship in residence there. But he had no wish to pursue a career as a <a href="/wiki/University_don" class="mw-redirect" title="University don">don</a>; the traditional route for politically ambitious but unmoneyed young men was through the law. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins25_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins25-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> While still at Oxford Asquith had already entered <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%27s_Inn" title="Lincoln&#39;s Inn">Lincoln's Inn</a> to train as a <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barrister</a>, and in 1875 he served a <a href="/wiki/Pupillage" title="Pupillage">pupillage</a> under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bowen,_1st_Baron_Bowen" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Bowen, 1st Baron Bowen">Charles Bowen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins27_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins27-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> He was <a href="/wiki/Call_to_the_bar" title="Call to the bar">called to the bar</a> in June 1876.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson36_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson36-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There followed what Jenkins calls "seven extremely lean years".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins27_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins27-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Asquith set up a legal practice with two other junior barristers. With no personal contacts with solicitors, he received few <a href="/wiki/Brief_(law)" title="Brief (law)">briefs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> Those that came his way he argued capably, but he was too fastidious to learn the wilier tricks of the legal trade: "he was constitutionally incapable of making a discreet fog&#160;… nor could he prevail on himself to dispense the conventional patter".<sup id="cite_ref-times2_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times2-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He did not allow his lack of money to stop him marrying. His bride, Helen Kelsall Melland (1854–1891), was the daughter of Frederick Melland, a physician in Manchester. She and Asquith had met through friends of his mother's.<sup id="cite_ref-times2_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times2-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The two had been in love for several years, but it was not until 1877 that Asquith sought her father's consent to their marriage. Despite Asquith's limited income—practically nothing from the bar and a small stipend from his fellowship—Melland consented after making inquiries about the young man's potential. Helen had a private income of several hundred pounds a year, and the couple lived in modest comfort in <a href="/wiki/Hampstead" title="Hampstead">Hampstead</a>. They had five children: 
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Raymond_Asquith" title="Raymond Asquith">Raymond Asquith</a> (6 November 1878 – 15 September 1916), who married <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Asquith" title="Katharine Asquith">Katharine Horner</a> (daughter of <a href="/wiki/John_Francis_Fortescue_Horner" title="John Francis Fortescue Horner">Sir John Horner</a>) on 25 July 1907. They had three children.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Asquith_(poet)" title="Herbert Asquith (poet)">Herbert Asquith</a> (11 March 1881 – 5 August 1947), who married Lady Cynthia Charteris (daughter of <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Charteris,_11th_Earl_of_Wemyss" title="Hugo Charteris, 11th Earl of Wemyss">Hugo Richard Charteris, 11th Earl of Wemyss and 7th Earl of March</a>) on 28 July 1910. They had three children.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Asquith" title="Arthur Asquith">Arthur Asquith</a> ( 24 April 1883 – 25 August 1939), who married Betty Constance Manners (daughter of <a href="/wiki/John_Manners-Sutton,_3rd_Baron_Manners" title="John Manners-Sutton, 3rd Baron Manners">John Manners-Sutton, 3rd Baron Manners</a>) on 30 April 1918. They had four daughters.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Violet_Bonham_Carter" title="Violet Bonham Carter">Violet Asquith</a> (15 April 1887 – 19 February 1969), who married Sir Maurice Bonham Carter on 30 November 1915. They had four children.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyril_Asquith,_Baron_Asquith_of_Bishopstone" title="Cyril Asquith, Baron Asquith of Bishopstone">Cyril Asquith, Baron Asquith of Bishopstone</a> (5 February 1890 – 24 August 1954),<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> who married Anne Pollock (daughter of Sir Adrian Donald Wilde Pollock) on 12 February 1918. They had four children.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f9/H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg/170px-H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f9/H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg/255px-H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f9/H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg/340px-H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="905" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:H-H-Asquith-1876.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith in 1876</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1876 and 1884 Asquith supplemented his income by writing regularly for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i>, which at that time had a broadly Liberal outlook. Matthew comments that the articles Asquith wrote for the magazine give a good overview of his political views as a young man. He was staunchly radical, but as unconvinced by extreme left-wing views as by <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Toryism</a>. Among the topics that caused debate among Liberals were British imperialism, the union of Great Britain and Ireland, and female suffrage. Asquith was a strong, though not jingoistic, proponent of the Empire, and, after initial caution, came to support home rule for Ireland. He opposed votes for women for most of his political career.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> There was also an element of party interest: Asquith believed that votes for women would disproportionately benefit the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a>. In a 2001 study of the extension of the franchise between 1832 and 1931, Bob Whitfield concluded that Asquith's surmise about the electoral impact was correct.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield228_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield228-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In addition to his work for <i>The Spectator</i>, he was retained as a <a href="/wiki/Editorial" title="Editorial">leader</a> writer by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>, taught at evening classes, and marked examination papers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins31–32_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins31–32-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's career as a barrister began to flourish in 1883 when <a href="/wiki/Robert_Samuel_Wright" title="Robert Samuel Wright">R. S. Wright</a> invited him to join his chambers at the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Temple" title="Inner Temple">Inner Temple</a>. Wright was the Junior Counsel to the Treasury, a post often known as "the <a href="/wiki/Law_Officers_of_the_Crown#England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Law Officers of the Crown">Attorney General</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Treasury_devil" class="mw-redirect" title="Treasury devil">devil</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-wright_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wright-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> whose function included giving legal advice to ministers and government departments.<sup id="cite_ref-wright_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wright-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> One of Asquith's first jobs in working for Wright was to prepare a memorandum for the prime minister, <a href="/wiki/W._E._Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="W. E. Gladstone">W. E. Gladstone</a>, on the status of the parliamentary oath in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bradlaugh" title="Charles Bradlaugh">Bradlaugh case</a>. Both Gladstone and the <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_for_England_and_Wales" title="Attorney General for England and Wales">Attorney General</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_James,_1st_Baron_James_of_Hereford" title="Henry James, 1st Baron James of Hereford">Sir Henry James</a>, were impressed. This raised Asquith's profile, though not greatly enhancing his finances. Much more remunerative were his new contacts with solicitors who regularly instructed Wright and now also began to instruct Asquith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins37_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins37-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Member_of_Parliament_and_Queen.27s_Counsel"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Parliament_and_Queen's_Counsel">Member of Parliament and Queen's Counsel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Member of Parliament and Queen&#039;s Counsel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 1886, with the Liberal party split on the question of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a>, Gladstone called <a href="/wiki/1886_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1886 United Kingdom general election">a general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas71_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas71-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> There was a last-minute vacancy at <a href="/wiki/East_Fife_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Fife (UK Parliament constituency)">East Fife</a>, where the sitting Liberal member, <a href="/wiki/John_Boyd_Kinnear" title="John Boyd Kinnear">John Boyd Kinnear</a>, had been deselected by his local Liberal Association for voting against Irish Home Rule. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Haldane,_1st_Viscount_Haldane" title="Richard Haldane, 1st Viscount Haldane">Richard Haldane</a>, a close friend of Asquith's and also a struggling young barrister, had been Liberal MP for the nearby <a href="/wiki/Haddingtonshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Haddingtonshire (UK Parliament constituency)">Haddingtonshire</a> constituency since <a href="/wiki/1885_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1885 United Kingdom general election">December 1885</a>. He put Asquith's name forward as a replacement for Kinnear, and only ten days before polling Asquith was formally nominated in a vote of the local Liberals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins38–40_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins38–40-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives did not contest the seat, putting their support behind Kinnear, who stood against Asquith as a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal Unionist">Liberal Unionist</a>. Asquith was elected with 2,863 votes to Kinnear's 2,489.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Liberals lost the 1886 election, and Asquith joined the House of Commons as an opposition backbencher. He waited until March 1887 to make his maiden speech, which opposed the Conservative administration's proposal to give special priority to an Irish Crimes Bill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith52_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith52-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson37–38_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson37–38-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> From the start of his parliamentary career Asquith impressed other MPs with his air of authority as well as his lucidity of expression.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins42–43_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins42–43-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> For the remainder of this Parliament, which lasted until 1892, Asquith spoke occasionally but effectively, mostly on Irish matters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson44_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson44-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins44_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins44-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's legal practice was flourishing, and took up much of his time. In the late 1880s <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Hope" title="Anthony Hope">Anthony Hope</a>, who later gave up the bar to become a novelist, was his pupil. Asquith disliked arguing in front of a jury because of the repetitiveness and "platitudes" required, but excelled at arguing fine points of civil law before a judge or in front of courts of appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith48_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith48-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> These cases, in which his clients were generally large businesses, were unspectacular but financially rewarding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins47_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins47-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg/170px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg/255px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg/340px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1695" data-file-height="2887" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_1_August_1891-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith, caricatured by <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Ward" title="Leslie Ward">Spy</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(British_magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vanity Fair (British magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i>, 1891</div></div></div>
<p>From time to time Asquith appeared in high-profile criminal cases. In 1887 and 1888 he defended the radical Liberal MP, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bontine_Cunninghame_Graham" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Bontine Cunninghame Graham">Cunninghame Graham</a>, who was charged with assaulting police officers when they attempted to break up <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1887)" title="Bloody Sunday (1887)">a demonstration</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Graham was later convicted of the lesser charge of <a href="/wiki/Unlawful_assembly" title="Unlawful assembly">unlawful assembly</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In what Jenkins calls "a less liberal cause", Asquith appeared for the prosecution in the trial of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Vizetelly" title="Henry Vizetelly">Henry Vizetelly</a> for publishing "obscene libels"—the first English versions of <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Zola" title="Émile Zola">Zola</a>'s novels <i><a href="/wiki/Nana_(novel)" title="Nana (novel)">Nana</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pot-Bouille" title="Pot-Bouille">Pot-Bouille</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/La_Terre" title="La Terre">La Terre</a></i>, which Asquith described in court as "the three most immoral books ever published".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's law career received a great and unforeseen boost in 1889 when he was named junior counsel to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Russell,_Baron_Russell_of_Killowen" title="Charles Russell, Baron Russell of Killowen">Sir Charles Russell</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Parnell_Commission" title="Parnell Commission">Parnell Commission of Enquiry</a>. The commission had been set up in the aftermath of damaging statements in <i>The Times</i>, based on forged letters, that Irish MP <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stuart_Parnell" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Stuart Parnell">Charles Stuart Parnell</a> had expressed approval of Dublin's <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_Park_killings" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenix Park killings">Phoenix Park killings</a>. When the manager of <i>The Times</i>, J. C. Macdonald, was called to give evidence Russell, feeling tired, surprised Asquith by asking him to conduct the cross-examination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson33_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson33-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Under Asquith's questioning, it became plain that in accepting the forgeries as genuine, without making any check, Macdonald had, in Jenkins's phrase, behaved "with a credulity which would have been childlike had it not been criminally negligent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins49_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins49-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Manchester_Guardian" class="mw-redirect" title="The Manchester Guardian">The Manchester Guardian</a></i> reported that under Asquith's cross-examination, Macdonald "squirmed and wriggled through a dozen half-formed phrases in an attempt at explanation, and finished none".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The accusations against Parnell were shown to be false, <i>The Times</i> was obliged to make a full apology, and Asquith's reputation was assured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopplewell24–25_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopplewell24–25-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson33–34_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson33–34-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Within a year he had gained advancement to the senior rank of the bar, <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Counsel" title="Queen&#39;s Counsel">Queen's Counsel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopplewell25_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopplewell25-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith appeared in two important cases in the early 1890s. He played an effective low-key role in the sensational <a href="/wiki/Royal_baccarat_scandal" title="Royal baccarat scandal">Tranby Croft libel</a> trial (1891), helping to show that the plaintiff had not been libelled. He was on the losing side in <i><a href="/wiki/Carlill_v_Carbolic_Smoke_Ball_Co" title="Carlill v Carbolic Smoke Ball Co">Carlill v Carbolic Smoke Ball Co</a></i> (1892), a landmark English contract law case that established that a company was obliged to meet its advertised pledges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopplewell28–30_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopplewell28–30-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Widower_and_cabinet_minister">Widower and cabinet minister</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Widower and cabinet minister">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In September 1891 Helen Asquith died of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> following a few days' illness while the family were on holiday in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins52_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins52-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Asquith bought a house in <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>, and hired nannies and other domestic staff. He sold the Hampstead property and took a flat in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Street,_London" title="Mount Street, London">Mount Street</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayfair" title="Mayfair">Mayfair</a>, where he lived during the working week.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins56_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins56-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margot-Asquith-1890s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Margot-Asquith-1890s.jpg/170px-Margot-Asquith-1890s.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4a/Margot-Asquith-1890s.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="243" data-file-height="343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margot-Asquith-1890s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Margot_Asquith" title="Margot Asquith">Margot Asquith</a> at about the time of her marriage</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1892_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1892 United Kingdom general election">general election of July 1892</a> returned Gladstone and the Liberals to office, with intermittent support from the Irish Nationalist MPs. Asquith, who was then only 39 and had never served as a junior minister, accepted the post of <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, a senior Cabinet position. The Conservatives and Liberal Unionists jointly outnumbered the Liberals in the Commons, which, together with a permanent Unionist majority in the House of Lords, restricted the government's capacity to put reforming measures in place. Asquith failed to secure a majority for a bill to <a href="/wiki/Disestablishmentarianism" title="Disestablishmentarianism">disestablish</a> the <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church of Wales</a>, and another to protect workers injured at work, but he built up a reputation as a capable and fair minister.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1893, Asquith responded to a request from Magistrates in the <a href="/wiki/Wakefield" title="Wakefield">Wakefield</a> area for reinforcements to police a mining strike. Asquith sent 400 Metropolitan policeman. After two civilians were killed in <a href="/wiki/Featherstone" title="Featherstone">Featherstone</a> when soldiers opened fire on a crowd, Asquith was subject to protests at public meetings for a period. He responded to a taunt, "Why did you murder the miners at Featherstone in '92?" by saying, "It was not '92, it was '93."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When Gladstone retired in March 1894, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> chose the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Secretary" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign Secretary">Foreign Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery" title="Archibald Primrose, 5th Earl of Rosebery">Lord Rosebery</a>, as the new prime minister. Asquith thought Rosebery preferable to the other possible candidate, the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Vernon_Harcourt_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Vernon Harcourt (politician)">Sir William Harcourt</a>, whom he deemed too anti-imperialist—one of the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Little_Englander#History" title="Little Englander">Little Englanders</a>"—and too abrasive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins72–73_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins72–73-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Asquith remained at the Home Office until the government fell in 1895.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith had known <a href="/wiki/Margot_Asquith" title="Margot Asquith">Margot Tennant</a> slightly since before his wife's death, and grew increasingly attached to her in his years as a widower. On 10 May 1894 they were married at <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s,_Hanover_Square" class="mw-redirect" title="St George&#39;s, Hanover Square">St George's, Hanover Square</a>. Asquith became a son in law of <a href="/wiki/Sir_Charles_Tennant,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Charles Tennant, 1st Baronet">Sir Charles Tennant, 1st Baronet</a>. Margot was in many respects the opposite of Asquith's first wife, being outgoing, impulsive, extravagant and opinionated.<sup id="cite_ref-margodnb_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-margodnb-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Despite the misgivings of many of Asquith's friends and colleagues the marriage proved to be a success. Margot got on, if sometimes stormily, with her step-children and she and Asquith had five children of their own, only two of whom survived infancy.:<sup id="cite_ref-margodnb_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-margodnb-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Anthony_Asquith" title="Anthony Asquith">Anthony Asquith</a> (9 November 1902 – 21 February 1968)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Bibesco" title="Elizabeth Bibesco">Elizabeth Asquith</a> (26 February 1897 – 7 April 1945), she married Prince Antoine Bibesco on 30 April 1919. They had one daughter.</li></ul>
<h3><span id="Out_of_office.2C_1895.E2.80.931905"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Out_of_office,_1895–1905">Out of office, 1895–1905</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Out of office, 1895–1905">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1895_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1895 United Kingdom general election">general election of July 1895</a> was disastrous for the Liberals, and the Conservatives under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> won a majority of 152. With no government post, Asquith divided his time between politics and the bar.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> Jenkins comments that in this period Asquith earned a substantial, though not stellar, income and was never worse off and often much higher-paid than when in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins92_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins92-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Matthew writes that his income as a QC in the following years was around £5,000 to £10,000 per annum (around £500,000–£1,000,000 at 2015 prices).<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> According to Haldane, on returning to government in 1905 Asquith had to give up a £10,000 brief to act for the <a href="/wiki/Abbas_II_of_Egypt" title="Abbas II of Egypt">Khedive of Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBates33_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBates33-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Margot later claimed (in the 1920s, when they were short of money) that he could have made £50,000 per annum had he remained at the bar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss282–283_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss282–283-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg/220px-Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg/330px-Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg/440px-Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="594" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Campbell-bannerman-ILN-1899.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Campbell-Bannerman</a>, Liberal leader from 1899</div></div></div>
<p>The Liberal Party, with a leadership—Harcourt in the Commons and Rosebery in the Lords—who detested each other, once again suffered factional divisions. Rosebery resigned in October 1896 and Harcourt followed him in December 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley60_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley60-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins200_and_105_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins200_and_105-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Asquith came under strong pressure to accept the nomination to take over as Liberal leader, but the post of Leader of the Opposition, though full-time, was then unpaid, and he could not afford to give up his income as a barrister. He and others prevailed on the former <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_War" title="Secretary of State for War">Secretary of State for War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a> to accept the post.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley65_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley65-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Boer_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer War">Boer War</a> of 1899–1902 Liberal opinion divided along pro-imperialist and "Little England" lines, with Campbell-Bannerman striving to maintain party unity. Asquith was less inclined than his leader and many in the party to censure the Conservative government for its conduct, though he regarded the war as an unnecessary distraction.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a>, a former Liberal minister, now an ally of the Conservatives, campaigned for <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">tariffs</a> to shield British industry from cheaper foreign competition. Asquith's advocacy of traditional Liberal <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> policies helped to make Chamberlain's proposals the central question in British politics in the early years of the 20th century. In Matthew's view, "Asquith's forensic skills quickly exposed deficiencies and self-contradictions in Chamberlain's arguments."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The question divided the Conservatives, while the Liberals were united under the banner of "free fooders" against those in the government who countenanced a tax on imported essentials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins140_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins140-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer.2C_1905.E2.80.931908"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer,_1905–1908">Chancellor of the Exchequer, 1905–1908</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Chancellor of the Exchequer, 1905–1908">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/47/Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg/220px-Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/47/Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg/330px-Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/47/Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg/440px-Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="578" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith-as-Chancellor-1907.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, in the House of Commons</div></div></div>
<p>Salisbury's Conservative successor as Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, resigned in December 1905, but did not seek a dissolution of Parliament and a general election.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">King Edward VII</a> invited Campbell-Bannerman to form a minority government. Asquith and his close political allies Haldane and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon" title="Edward Grey, 1st Viscount Grey of Fallodon">Sir Edward Grey</a> tried to pressure him into taking a peerage to become a figurehead Prime Minister in the House of Lords, giving the pro-empire wing of the party greater dominance in the House of Commons. Campbell-Bannerman called their bluff and refused to move.<sup id="cite_ref-morris_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morris-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins155_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins155-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was appointed Chancellor of the Exchequer. He held the post for over two years, and introduced three budgets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith172–173_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith172–173-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins158_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins158-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A month after taking office, Campbell-Bannerman called <a href="/wiki/1906_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1906 United Kingdom general election">a general election</a>, in which the Liberals gained a landslide majority of 132.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins164_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins164-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> However, Asquith's first budget, in 1906, was constrained by the annual income and expenditure plans he had inherited from his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Austen_Chamberlain" title="Austen Chamberlain">Austen Chamberlain</a>. The only income for which Chamberlain had over-budgeted was the duty from sales of alcohol.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins161_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins161-84">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> With a balanced budget, and a realistic assessment of future public expenditure, Asquith was able, in his second and third budgets, to lay the foundations for limited redistribution of wealth and welfare provisions for the poor. Blocked at first by Treasury officials from setting a variable rate of income tax with higher rates on those with high incomes, he set up a committee under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dilke" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Dilke">Sir Charles Dilke</a> which recommended not only variable income tax rates but also a <a href="/wiki/Supertax" class="mw-redirect" title="Supertax">supertax</a> on incomes of more than £5,000 a year. Asquith also introduced a distinction between earned and unearned income, taxing the latter at a higher rate. He used the increased revenues to fund old-age pensions, the first time a British government had provided them. Reductions in selective taxes, such as that on sugar, were aimed at benefiting the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins162–164_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins162–164-85">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith planned the 1908 budget, but by the time he presented it to the Commons he was no longer Chancellor. Campbell-Bannerman's health had been failing for nearly a year. After a series of heart attacks he resigned on 3 April 1908, less than three weeks before he died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley132–136_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley132–136-86">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was universally accepted as the natural successor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas123_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas123-87">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> King Edward, who was on holiday in <a href="/wiki/Biarritz" title="Biarritz">Biarritz</a>, sent for Asquith, who took the boat train to France and <a href="/wiki/Kissing_hands" title="Kissing hands">kissed hands</a> as prime minister in the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_du_Palais" title="Hôtel du Palais">Hôtel du Palais</a>, Biarritz on 8 April.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins179–180_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins179–180-88">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Peacetime_prime_minister:_1908.E2.80.931914"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Peacetime_prime_minister:_1908–1914">Peacetime prime minister: 1908–1914</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Peacetime prime minister: 1908–1914">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_government,_1905%E2%80%931915" title="Liberal government, 1905–1915">Liberal government, 1905–1915</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointments_and_cabinet">Appointments and cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Appointments and cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg/170px-Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg/255px-Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg/340px-Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3209" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert-Henry-Asquith-1st-Earl-of-Oxford-and-Asquith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith in 1908</div></div></div>
<p>On Asquith's return from Biarritz, his leadership of the Liberals was affirmed by a party meeting (the first time this had been done for a prime minister).<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He initiated a cabinet reshuffle. Lloyd George was promoted to be Asquith's replacement as chancellor. <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> succeeded Lloyd George as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Board_of_Trade" title="President of the Board of Trade">President of the Board of Trade</a>, entering the Cabinet despite his youth (aged 33) and the fact that he had <a href="/wiki/Crossing_the_floor" title="Crossing the floor">crossed the floor</a> to become a Liberal only four years previously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins181_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins181-89">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith demoted or dismissed a number of Campbell-Bannerman's cabinet ministers. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Marjoribanks,_2nd_Baron_Tweedmouth" title="Edward Marjoribanks, 2nd Baron Tweedmouth">Lord Tweedmouth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Admiralty" title="First Lord of the Admiralty">First Lord of the Admiralty</a>, was relegated to the nominal post of <a href="/wiki/Lord_President_of_the_Council" title="Lord President of the Council">Lord President of the Council</a>. <a href="/wiki/Victor_Bruce,_9th_Earl_of_Elgin" title="Victor Bruce, 9th Earl of Elgin">Lord Elgin</a> was sacked from the Colonial Office and the Earl of Portsmouth (whom Asquith had tutored) was too, as undersecretary at the War Office. The abruptness of their dismissals caused hard feelings; Elgin wrote to Tweedmouth, "I venture to think that even a prime minister may have some regard for the usages common among gentlemen&#160;… I feel that even a housemaid gets a better warning."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst504–505_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst504–505-91">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian Cameron Hazlehurst wrote that "the new men, with the old, made a powerful team".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst506_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst506-92">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The cabinet choices balanced the competing factions in the party; the appointments of Lloyd George and Churchill satisfied the radicals, while the whiggish element favoured <a href="/wiki/Reginald_McKenna" title="Reginald McKenna">Reginald McKenna</a>'s appointment as First Lord.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_minister_at_leisure">Prime minister at leisure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Prime minister at leisure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Possessed of "a faculty for working quickly",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985470_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985470-93">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Asquith had considerable time for leisure. Reading<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss93_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss93-94">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> the classics, poetry and a vast range of English literature consumed much of his time. So did correspondence; intensely disliking the telephone, Asquith was a prolific letter writer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_198513_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_198513-95">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Travelling, often to country houses owned by members of Margot's family, was almost constant, Asquith being a devoted "<a href="/wiki/Workweek_and_weekend" title="Workweek and weekend">weekender</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins259–261_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins259–261-96">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> He spent part of each summer in Scotland, with golf, constituency matters, and time at <a href="/wiki/Balmoral_Castle" title="Balmoral Castle">Balmoral</a> as duty minister.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He and Margot divided their time between Downing Street and <a href="/wiki/Mill_House_and_The_Wharf,_Sutton_Courtenay" title="Mill House and The Wharf, Sutton Courtenay">The Wharf</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyack,_Bradley_&amp;_Pevsner553_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyack,_Bradley_&amp;_Pevsner553-97">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> a country house at <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Courtenay" title="Sutton Courtenay">Sutton Courtenay</a> in Berkshire which they bought in 1912;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins259_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins259-98">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> their London mansion, 20 <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Square" title="Cavendish Square">Cavendish Square</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014xli_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014xli-99">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> was let during his premiership. He was addicted to <a href="/wiki/Contract_bridge" title="Contract bridge">Contract bridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss94_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss94-100">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Above all else, Asquith thrived on company and conversation. A clubbable man, he enjoyed "the companionship of clever and attractive women" even more.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985471_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985471-101">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Throughout his life, Asquith had a circle of close female friends, which Margot termed his "harem".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014xlviii_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014xlviii-102">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In 1912, one of these, <a href="/wiki/Venetia_Stanley_(1887%E2%80%931948)" title="Venetia Stanley (1887–1948)">Venetia Stanley</a> became much closer. Meeting first in 1909–1910, by 1912 she was Asquith's constant correspondent and companion. Between that point and 1915, he wrote her some 560 letters, at a rate of up to four a day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985preface_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985preface-103">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Although it remains uncertain whether or not they were lovers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_19853_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_19853-104">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> she became of central importance to him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss140_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss140-105">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's thorough enjoyment of "comfort and luxury"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985471_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985471-101">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> during peacetime, and his unwillingness to adjust his behaviour during conflict,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014xcv_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014xcv-106">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> ultimately contributed to the impression of a man out of touch. <a href="/wiki/Helen_Maud_Holt" title="Helen Maud Holt">Lady Tree</a>'s teasing question, asked at the height of the conflict; "Tell me, Mr Asquith, do you take an interest in the war;"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdelman11_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdelman11-107">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> conveyed a commonly held view.
</p><p>Asquith enjoyed alcohol and his drinking was the subject of considerable gossip. His relaxed attitude to drink disappointed the temperance element in the Liberal coalition<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and some authors have suggested it affected his decision-making, for example in his opposition to Lloyd George's wartime attacks on the liquor trade.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The Conservative leader Bonar Law quipped, "Asquith drunk can make a better speech than any of us sober".
<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> His reputation suffered, especially as wartime crises demanded the full alert attention of the prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Owen" title="David Owen">David Owen</a> writes that Asquith was ordered by his doctor to rein in his consumption after a near-collapse in April 1911, but it is unclear whether he actually did so. Owen, a medical doctor by training, states that "by modern diagnostic standards, Asquith became an alcoholic while Prime Minister." Witnesses often remarked on his weight gain and red, bloated face.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy">Domestic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Domestic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforming_the_House_of_Lords">Reforming the House of Lords</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reforming the House of Lords">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Asquith hoped to act as a mediator between members of his cabinet as they pushed Liberal legislation through Parliament. Events, including conflict with the House of Lords, forced him to the front from the start of his premiership. Despite the Liberals's massive majority in the House of Commons, the Tories had overwhelming support in the unelected <a href="/wiki/Upper_chamber" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper chamber">upper chamber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith239_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith239-113">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup> Campbell-Bannerman had favoured reforming the Lords by providing that a bill thrice passed by the Commons at least six months apart could become law without the Lords' consent, while diminishing the power of the Commons by reducing the maximum term of a parliament from seven to five years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeston508_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeston508-115">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Asquith, as chancellor, had served on a cabinet committee that had written a plan to resolve legislative stalemates by a joint sitting of the Commons as a body with 100 of the peers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeston508–512_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeston508–512-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The Commons passed a number of pieces of legislation in 1908 which were defeated or heavily amended in the Lords, including a Licensing Bill, a Scottish Small Landholders' Bill, and a Scottish Land Values Bill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith239_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith239-113">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
