</p><p>None of these bills were important enough to dissolve parliament and seek a new mandate at a general election.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Asquith and Lloyd George believed the peers would back down if presented with Liberal objectives contained within a finance bill—the Lords had not obstructed a money bill since the 17th century, and after initially blocking <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">Gladstone</a>'s attempt (as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">chancellor</a>) to repeal Paper Duties, had yielded in 1861 when it was submitted again in a finance bill. Accordingly, the Liberal leadership expected that after much objection from the Tory peers, the Lords would yield to policy changes wrapped within a budget bill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss112_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss112-117">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="1909:_People.27s_Budget"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1909:_People's_Budget">1909: People's Budget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 1909: People&#039;s Budget">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg/220px-Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg/330px-Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg/440px-Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1348" data-file-height="1202" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This 1909 <i>Punch</i> cartoon suggests the Liberals were delighted when the Lords forced an election. Back row: Haldane, Churchill with arms up, being hugged by his ally Lloyd George. Asquith standing at right. Bottom row: McKenna, Lord Crewe (with moustache), Augustine Birrell leaning back</div></div></div>
<p>In a major speech in December 1908, Asquith announced that the upcoming budget would reflect the Liberals' policy agenda, and the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People's Budget</a> that was submitted to Parliament by Lloyd George the following year greatly expanded <a href="/wiki/Social_welfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Social welfare">social welfare</a> programmes. To pay for them, it significantly increased both <a href="/wiki/Direct_tax" title="Direct tax">direct</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indirect_tax" title="Indirect tax">indirect</a> taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> These included a 20 per cent tax on the unearned increase in value in land, payable at death of the owner or sale of the land. There would also be a tax of &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>d in the pound<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup> on undeveloped land. A graduated <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a> was imposed, and there were increases in imposts on tobacco, beer and spirits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith254–255_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith254–255-119">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> A tax on petrol was introduced despite Treasury concerns that it could not work in practice. Although Asquith held fourteen cabinet meetings to assure unity amongst his ministers,<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> there was opposition from some Liberals; Rosebery described the budget as "inquisitorial, tyrannical, and Socialistic".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins199_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins199-120">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The budget divided the country and provoked bitter debate through the summer of 1909.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins198–199_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins198–199-121">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Harmsworth,_1st_Viscount_Northcliffe" title="Alfred Harmsworth, 1st Viscount Northcliffe">Northcliffe</a> Press (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i>) urged rejection of the budget to give tariff reform (indirect taxes on imported goods which, it was felt, would encourage British industry and trade within the Empire) a chance; there were many public meetings, some of them organised by <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">dukes</a>, in protest at the budget.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagnus_1964232,_527_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagnus_1964232,_527-122">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Many Liberal politicians attacked the peers, including Lloyd George in his <a href="/wiki/Limehouse" title="Limehouse">Limehouse</a> speech, in which he said "a fully-equipped Duke costs as much to keep up as two <a href="/wiki/Dreadnought" title="Dreadnought">dreadnoughts</a> (battleships)" and was "less easy to scrap".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeffer276–277_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeffer276–277-123">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> King Edward privately urged Conservative leaders Balfour and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Petty-Fitzmaurice,_5th_Marquess_of_Lansdowne" title="Henry Petty-Fitzmaurice, 5th Marquess of Lansdowne">Lord Lansdowne</a> to pass the Budget (this was not unusual, as <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> had helped to broker agreement between the two Houses over the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Church_Act_1869" title="Irish Church Act 1869">Irish Church Act 1869</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1884" title="Representation of the People Act 1884">Third Reform Act in 1884</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeffer281–282_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeffer281–282-124">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> From July it became increasingly clear that the Tory peers would reject the budget, partly in the hope of forcing an election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagnus_1964534_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagnus_1964534-125">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> If they rejected it, Asquith determined, he would have to ask the King to dissolve Parliament, four years into a seven-year term,<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> as it would mean the legislature had refused <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">supply</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup> The budget passed the Commons on 4 November 1909, but was voted down in the Lords on the 30th, the Lords passing a resolution by Lord Lansdowne stating that they were entitled to oppose the finance bill as it lacked an electoral mandate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeffer283–284_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeffer283–284-127">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Asquith had Parliament <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session#United_Kingdom" title="Legislative session">prorogued</a> three days later for an election beginning on 15 January 1910, with the Commons first passing a resolution deeming the Lords' vote to be an attack on the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss116–117_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss116–117-128">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1910:_election_and_constitutional_deadlock">1910: election and constitutional deadlock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 1910: election and constitutional deadlock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/January_1910_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="January 1910 United Kingdom general election">January 1910 general election</a> was dominated by talk of removing the Lords' veto.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> A possible solution was to threaten to have King Edward pack the House of Lords with freshly minted Liberal peers, who would override the Lords's veto; Asquith's talk of safeguards was taken by many to mean that he had secured the King's agreement to this. They were mistaken; the King had informed Asquith that he would not consider a mass creation of peers until after a <i>second</i> general election.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George and Churchill were the leading forces in the Liberals' appeal to the voters; Asquith, clearly tired, took to the hustings for a total of two weeks during the campaign, and when the polls began, journeyed to <a href="/wiki/Cannes" title="Cannes">Cannes</a> with such speed that he neglected an engagement with the King, to the monarch's annoyance. The result was a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>. The Liberals lost heavily from their great majority of 1906, but still finished with two more seats than the Conservatives. With <a href="/wiki/Nationalist_Party_(Ireland)" title="Nationalist Party (Ireland)">Irish Nationalist</a> and Labour support, the government would have ample support on most issues, and Asquith stated that his majority compared favourably with those enjoyed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Palmerston</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Lord John Russell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss118_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss118-130">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg/170px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg/255px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg/340px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1918" data-file-height="3200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith_Vanity_Fair_17_March_1910.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith caricatured in <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(British_magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vanity Fair (British magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i>, 1910</div></div></div>
<p>Immediate further pressure to remove the Lords' veto now came from the Irish MPs, who wanted to remove the Lords' ability to block the introduction of Irish Home Rule. They threatened to vote against the Budget unless they had their way.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagnus_1964548_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagnus_1964548-131">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup> With another general election likely before long, Asquith had to make clear the Liberal policy on constitutional change to the country without alienating the Irish and Labour. This initially proved difficult, and the King's speech opening Parliament was vague on what was to be done to neutralise the Lords' veto. Asquith dispirited his supporters by stating in Parliament that he had neither asked for nor received a commitment from the King to create peers.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The cabinet considered resigning and leaving it up to Balfour to try to form a Conservative government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeffer290–293_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeffer290–293-133">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The budget passed the Commons again, and—now that it had an electoral mandate—it was approved by the Lords in April without a division.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss121_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss121-134">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The cabinet finally decided to back a plan based on Campbell-Bannerman's, that a bill passed by the Commons in three consecutive annual sessions would become law notwithstanding the Lords' objections. Unless the King guaranteed that he would create enough Liberal peers to pass the bill, ministers would resign and allow Balfour to form a government, leaving the matter to be debated at the ensuing general election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins208–210_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins208–210-135">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> On 14 April 1910, the Commons passed resolutions that would become the basis of the eventual <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a>: to remove the power of the Lords to veto money bills, to reduce blocking of other bills to a two-year power of delay, and also to reduce the term of a parliament from seven years to five.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeffer286–288_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeffer286–288-136">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In that debate Asquith also hinted—in part to ensure the support of the Irish MPs—that he would ask the King to break the deadlock "in that Parliament" (i.e. that he would ask for the mass creation of peers, contrary to the King's earlier stipulation that there be a second election).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeffer293_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeffer293-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;m&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These plans were scuttled by the death of Edward VII on 6 May 1910. Asquith and his ministers were initially reluctant to press the new king, <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">George V</a>, in mourning for his father, for commitments on constitutional change, and the monarch's views were not yet known. With a strong feeling in the country that the parties should compromise, Asquith and other Liberals met with Conservative leaders in a number of conferences through much of the remainder of 1910. These talks failed in November over Conservative insistence that there be no limits on the Lords's ability to veto Irish Home Rule.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> When the Parliament Bill was submitted to the Lords, they made amendments that were not acceptable to the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith298–299_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith298–299-139">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span id="1910.E2.80.931911:_second_election_and_Parliament_Act"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1910–1911:_second_election_and_Parliament_Act">1910–1911: second election and Parliament Act</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 1910–1911: second election and Parliament Act">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg/300px-1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="417" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/41/1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="414" data-file-height="575" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Punch</i> 1911 cartoon shows Asquith and Lloyd George preparing coronets for 500 new peers</div></div></div>
<p>On 11 November, Asquith asked King George to dissolve Parliament for <a href="/wiki/December_1910_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="December 1910 United Kingdom general election">another general election in December</a>, and on the 14th met again with the King and demanded assurances the monarch would create an adequate number of Liberal peers to carry the Parliament Bill. The King was slow to agree, and Asquith and his cabinet informed him they would resign if he did not make the commitment. Balfour had told King Edward that he would form a Conservative government if the Liberals left office but the new King did not know this. The King reluctantly gave in to Asquith's demand, writing in his diary that, "I disliked having to do this very much, but agreed that this was the only alternative to the Cabinet resigning, which at this moment would be disastrous".<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith dominated the short election campaign, focusing on the Lords' veto in calm speeches, compared by his biographer Stephen Koss to the "wild irresponsibility" of other major campaigners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss125_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss125-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In a speech at <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Hull</a>, he stated that the Liberals' purpose was to remove the obstruction, not establish an ideal upper house, "I have always got to deal—the country has got to deal—with things here and now. We need an instrument [of constitutional change] that can be set to work at once, which will get rid of deadlocks, and give us the fair and even chance in legislation to which we are entitled, and which is all that we demand."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith299–300_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith299–300-142">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill,_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg/220px-Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg/330px-Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg/440px-Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1554" data-file-height="932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill,_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Samuel Begg's depiction of the passing of the Parliament Bill in the House of Lords, 1911</div></div></div>
<p>The election resulted in little change to the party strengths (the Liberal and Conservative parties were exactly equal in size; by 1914 the Conservative Party would actually be larger owing to by-election victories). Nevertheless, Asquith remained in <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">Number Ten</a>, with a large majority in the Commons on the issue of the House of Lords. The Parliament Bill again passed the House of Commons in April 1911, and was heavily amended in the Lords. Asquith advised King George that the monarch would be called upon to create the peers, and the King agreed, asking that his pledge be made public, and that the Lords be allowed to reconsider their opposition. Once it was, there was a raging internal debate within the Tory party on whether to give in, or to continue to vote no even when outnumbered by hundreds of newly created peers. After lengthy debate, on 10 August 1911 the Lords voted narrowly not to insist on their amendments, with many Tory peers abstaining and a few voting in favour of the government; the bill was passed into law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins222–230_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins222–230-143">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Jenkins, although Asquith had at times moved slowly during the crisis, "on the whole, Asquith's slow moulding of events had amounted to a masterly display of political nerve and patient determination. Compared with [the Conservatives], his leadership was outstanding."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins231_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins231-144">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Churchill wrote to Asquith after the second 1910 election, "your leadership was the main and conspicuous feature of the whole fight".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss125_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss125-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Matthew, in his article on Asquith, found that, "the episode was the zenith of Asquith's prime ministerial career. In the British Liberal tradition, he patched rather than reformulated the constitution."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Social.2C_religious_and_labour_matters"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Social,_religious_and_labour_matters">Social, religious and labour matters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Social, religious and labour matters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Despite the distraction of the problem of the House of Lords, Asquith and his government moved ahead with a number of pieces of reforming legislation. According to Matthews, "no peacetime premier has been a more effective enabler. Labour exchanges, the introduction of unemployment and health insurance&#160;… reflected the reforms the government was able to achieve despite the problem of the Lords. Asquith was not himself a 'new Liberal', but he saw the need for a change in assumptions about the individual's relationship to the state, and he was fully aware of the political risk to the Liberals of a Labour Party on its left flank."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Keen to keep the support of the Labour Party, the Asquith government passed bills urged by that party, including the <a href="/wiki/Trade_Union_Act_1913" title="Trade Union Act 1913">Trade Union Act 1913</a> (reversing the <a href="/wiki/Osborne_judgment" class="mw-redirect" title="Osborne judgment">Osborne judgment</a>) and in 1911 granting MPs a salary, making it more feasible for working-class people to serve in the House of Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss230_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss230-145">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith had as chancellor placed money aside for the provision of non-contributory <a href="/wiki/Old-age_pension" class="mw-redirect" title="Old-age pension">old-age pensions</a>; the bill authorising them passed in 1908, during his premiership, despite some objection in the Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins166–167,_188_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins166–167,_188-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Jenkins noted that the scheme (which provided five shillings a week to single pensioners aged seventy and over, and slightly less than twice that to married couples) "to modern ears sounds cautious and meagre. But it was violently criticised at the time for showing a reckless generosity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins167_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins167-147">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's new government became embroiled in a controversy over the <a href="/wiki/Eucharistic_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Eucharistic Congress">Eucharistic Congress</a> of 1908, held in London. Following the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" title="Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829">Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> had seen a resurgence in Britain, and a large procession displaying the <a href="/wiki/Blessed_Sacrament" title="Blessed Sacrament">Blessed Sacrament</a> was planned to allow the laity to participate. Although such an event was forbidden by the 1829 act, planners counted on the British reputation for religious tolerance,<sup id="cite_ref-Devlin_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devlin-148">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Cardinal_Bourne" class="mw-redirect" title="Francis Cardinal Bourne">Francis Cardinal Bourne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Westminster" title="Archbishop of Westminster">Archbishop of Westminster</a>, had obtained permission from the Metropolitan Police. When the plans became widely known, King Edward objected, as did many other Protestants. Asquith received inconsistent advice from his Home Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert Gladstone">Herbert Gladstone</a>, and successfully pressed the organisers to cancel the religious aspects of the procession, though it cost him the resignation of his only Catholic cabinet minister, <a href="/wiki/George_Robinson,_1st_Marquess_of_Ripon" title="George Robinson, 1st Marquess of Ripon">Lord Ripon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins190–193_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins190–193-149">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Disestablishment of the Welsh Church was a Liberal priority, but despite support by most Welsh MPs, there was opposition in the Lords. Asquith was an authority on Welsh disestablishment from his time under Gladstone, but had little to do with the passage of <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Church_Act_1914" title="Welsh Church Act 1914">the bill</a>. It was twice rejected by the Lords, in 1912 and 1913, but having been forced through under the Parliament Act received royal assent in September 1914, with the provisions <a href="/wiki/Suspensory_Act_1914" title="Suspensory Act 1914">suspended</a> until war's end.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith356_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith356-150">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Votes_for_women">Votes for women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Votes for women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_(Nancy).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_%28Nancy%29.jpg/220px-Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_%28Nancy%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_%28Nancy%29.jpg/330px-Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_%28Nancy%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_%28Nancy%29.jpg/440px-Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_%28Nancy%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Votes_for_Women_lapel_pin_(Nancy).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 20th century suffragist lapel pin</div></div></div>
<p>Asquith had opposed votes for women as early as 1882, and he remained well known as an adversary throughout his time as prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss131_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss131-151">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> He took a detached view of the women's suffrage question, believing it should be judged on whether extending the franchise would improve the system of government, rather than as a question of rights. He did not understand—Jenkins ascribed it to a failure of imagination—why passions were raised on both sides over the issue. He told the House of Commons in 1913, while complaining of the "exaggerated language" on both sides, "I am sometimes tempted to think, as one listens to the arguments of supporters of women's suffrage, that there is nothing to be said for it, and I am sometimes tempted to think, as I listen to the arguments of the opponents of women's suffrage, that there is nothing to be said against it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins247_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins247-152">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1906 suffragettes <a href="/wiki/Annie_Kenney" title="Annie Kenney">Annie Kenney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_Knight" title="Adelaide Knight">Adelaide Knight</a>, and Mrs.<a href="/wiki/Jane_Sbarborough" title="Jane Sbarborough">Sbarborough</a> were arrested when they tried to obtain an audience with Asquith.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-153">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Taylor2014_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2014-154">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Offered either six weeks in prison or giving up campaigning for one year, the women all chose prison.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-153">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was a target for militant suffragettes as they abandoned hope of achieving the vote through peaceful means. He was several times the subject of their tactics: confronted (to his annoyance) at evening parties, accosted on the golf course, and ambushed while driving to <a href="/wiki/Stirling,_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Stirling, Scotland">Stirling</a> to dedicate a memorial to Campbell-Bannerman. On the last occasion, his top hat proved adequate protection against the dog whips wielded by the women. These incidents left him unmoved, as he did not believe them a true manifestation of public opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins247–248_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins247–248-155">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With a growing majority of the Cabinet, including Lloyd George and Churchill, in favour of <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">women's suffrage</a>, Asquith was pressed to allow consideration of a <a href="/wiki/Private_member%27s_bill" title="Private member&#39;s bill">private member's bill</a> to give women the vote. The majority of Liberal MPs were also in favour.<sup id="cite_ref-plot_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plot-156">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Jenkins deemed him one of the two main prewar obstacles to women gaining the vote, the other being the suffragists's own militancy. In 1912, Asquith reluctantly agreed to permit a free vote on an amendment to a pending reform bill, allowing women the vote on the same terms as men. This would have satisfied Liberal suffrage supporters, and many suffragists, but the Speaker in January 1913 ruled that the amendment changed the nature of the bill, which would have to be withdrawn. Asquith was loud in his complaints against the Speaker, but was privately relieved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins248–250_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins248–250-157">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith belatedly came around to support women's suffrage in 1917, by which time he was out of office.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Women over the age of thirty were eventually given the vote by Lloyd George's government under the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a>. Asquith's reforms to the House of Lords eased the way for the passage of the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_Home_Rule">Irish Home Rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Irish Home Rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force,_Belfast,_Northern_Ireland,_1914_Q81771.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force%2C_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1914_Q81771.jpg/220px-The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force%2C_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1914_Q81771.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force%2C_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1914_Q81771.jpg/330px-The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force%2C_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1914_Q81771.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force%2C_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1914_Q81771.jpg/440px-The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force%2C_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1914_Q81771.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5218" data-file-height="3952" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Ulster_Volunteer_Force,_Belfast,_Northern_Ireland,_1914_Q81771.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force" title="Ulster Volunteer Force">Ulster Volunteer Force</a> march through Belfast, 1914</div></div></div>
<p>As a minority party after 1910 elections, the Liberals depended on the Irish vote, controlled by <a href="/wiki/John_Redmond" title="John Redmond">John Redmond</a>. To gain Irish support for the budget and the parliament bill, Asquith promised Redmond that <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a> would be the highest priority.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> It proved much more complex and time-consuming than expected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad30_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad30-161">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Support for self-government for Ireland had been a tenet of the Liberal Party since 1886, but Asquith had not been as enthusiastic, stating in 1903 (while in opposition) that the party should never take office if that government would be dependent for survival on the support of the Irish Nationalist Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley184–185_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley184–185-162">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> After 1910, though, Irish Nationalist votes were essential to stay in power. Retaining Ireland in the Union was the declared intent of all parties, and the Nationalists, as part of the majority that kept Asquith in office, were entitled to seek enactment of their plans for Home Rule, and to expect Liberal and Labour support.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives, with die-hard support from the Protestant Orangemen of Ulster, were strongly opposed to Home Rule. The desire to retain a veto for the Lords on such bills had been an unbridgeable gap between the parties in the constitutional talks prior to the second 1910 election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins215_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins215-163">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cabinet committee (not including Asquith) that in 1911 planned the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_Act_1914" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule Act 1914">Third Home Rule Bill</a> opposed any special status for Protestant Ulster within majority-Catholic Ireland. Asquith later (in 1913) wrote to Churchill, stating that the Prime Minister had always believed and stated that the price of Home Rule should be a special status for Ulster. In spite of this, the bill as introduced in April 1912 contained no such provision, and was meant to apply to all Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Neither partition nor a special status for Ulster was likely to satisfy either side.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad30_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad30-161">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> The self-government offered by the bill was very limited, but Irish Nationalists, expecting Home Rule to come by gradual parliamentary steps, favoured it. The Conservatives and Irish Unionists opposed it. Unionists began preparing to get their way by force if necessary, prompting nationalist emulation. The Unionists were in general better financed and more organised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley215–218_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley215–218-164">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the Parliament Act the Unionists could no longer block Home Rule in the House of Lords, but only delay Royal Assent by two years. Asquith decided to postpone any concessions to the Unionists until the bill's third passage through the Commons, when he believed the Unionists would be desperate for a compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad30–31_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad30–31-165">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Jenkins concluded that had Asquith tried for an earlier agreement, he would have had no luck, as many of his opponents wanted a fight and the opportunity to smash his government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins281_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins281-166">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carson" title="Edward Carson">Edward Carson</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/University_of_Dublin_(constituency)" title="University of Dublin (constituency)">Dublin University</a> and leader of the Irish Unionists in Parliament, threatened a revolt if Home Rule was enacted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins274_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins274-167">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The new Conservative leader, <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>, campaigned in Parliament and in northern Ireland, warning Ulstermen against "Rome Rule", that is, domination by the island's Catholic majority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss134–135_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss134–135-168">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Many who opposed Home Rule felt that the Liberals had violated the Constitution—by pushing through major constitutional change without a clear electoral mandate, with the House of Lords, formerly the "watchdog of the constitution", not reformed as had been promised in the preamble of the 1911 Act—and thus justified actions that in other circumstances might be treason.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley190_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley190-169">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The passions generated by the Irish question contrasted with Asquith's cool detachment, and he wrote about the prospective partition of the county of <a href="/wiki/County_Tyrone" title="County Tyrone">Tyrone</a>, which had a mixed population, deeming it "an impasse, with unspeakable consequences, upon a matter which to English eyes seems inconceivably small, &amp; to Irish eyes immeasurably big".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad31_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad31-170">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> As the Commons debated the Home Rule bill in late 1912 and early 1913, unionists in the north of Ireland mobilised, with talk of Carson declaring a Provisional Government and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force" title="Ulster Volunteer Force">Ulster Volunteer Forces</a> (UVF) built around the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Lodge" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange Lodge">Orange Lodges</a>, but in the cabinet, only Churchill viewed this with alarm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley192–193_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley192–193-171">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> These forces, insisting on their loyalty to the British Crown but increasingly well-armed with smuggled German weapons, prepared to do battle with the British Army, but Unionist leaders were confident that the army would not aid in forcing Home Rule on Ulster.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad31_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad31-170">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> As the Home Rule bill awaited its third passage through the Commons, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Curragh_incident" title="Curragh incident">Curragh incident</a> occurred in April 1914. With deployment of troops into Ulster imminent and threatening language by Churchill and the Secretary of State for War, <a href="/wiki/J._E._B._Seely,_1st_Baron_Mottistone" title="J. E. B. Seely, 1st Baron Mottistone">John Seely</a>, around sixty army officers, led by Brigadier-General <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Gough" title="Hubert Gough">Hubert Gough</a>, announced that they would rather be dismissed from the service than obey.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> With unrest spreading to army officers in England, the Cabinet acted to placate the officers with a statement written by Asquith reiterating the duty of officers to obey lawful orders but claiming that the incident had been a misunderstanding. Seely then added an unauthorised assurance, countersigned by <a href="/wiki/John_French,_1st_Earl_of_Ypres" title="John French, 1st Earl of Ypres">Sir John French</a> (the professional head of the army), that the government had no intention of using force against Ulster. Asquith repudiated the addition, and required Seely and French to resign, taking on the War Office himself,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins311–313_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins311–313-172">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> retaining the additional responsibility until hostilities against Germany began.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad27_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad27-173">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within a month of the start of Asquith's tenure at the War Office, the UVF <a href="/wiki/Larne_gun-running" title="Larne gun-running">landed a large cargo of guns and ammunition</a> at <a href="/wiki/Larne" title="Larne">Larne</a>, but the Cabinet did not deem it prudent to arrest their leaders. On 12 May, Asquith announced that he would secure Home Rule's third passage through the Commons (accomplished on 25 May), but that there would be an amending bill with it, making special provision for Ulster. But the Lords made changes to the amending bill unacceptable to Asquith, and with no way to invoke the Parliament Act on the amending bill, Asquith agreed to meet other leaders at an all-party conference on 21 July at Buckingham Palace, chaired by the King. When no solution could be found, Asquith and his cabinet planned further concessions to the Unionists, but this did not occur as the crisis on the Continent erupted into war.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In September 1914, after the outbreak of the conflict, Asquith announced that the Home Rule bill would go on the statute book (as the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1914" title="Government of Ireland Act 1914">Government of Ireland Act 1914</a>) but would not go into force until after the war; in the interim a bill granting special status to Ulster would be considered. This solution satisfied neither side.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwen111–112_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwen111–112-174">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_and_defence_policy">Foreign and defence policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Foreign and defence policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Asquith led a deeply divided Liberal Party as Prime Minister, not least on questions of foreign relations and defence spending.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Under Balfour, Britain and France had agreed upon the <a href="/wiki/Entente_Cordiale" title="Entente Cordiale">Entente Cordiale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss143_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss143-175">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In 1906, at the time the Liberals took office, there was <a href="/wiki/First_Moroccan_Crisis" title="First Moroccan Crisis">an ongoing crisis between France and Germany</a> over Morocco, and the French asked for British help in the event of conflict. Grey, the Foreign Secretary, refused any formal arrangement, but gave it as his personal opinion that in the event of war Britain would aid France. France then asked for military conversations aimed at co-ordination in such an event. Grey agreed, and these went on in the following years, without cabinet knowledge—Asquith most likely did not know of them until 1911. When he learned of them, Asquith was concerned that the French took for granted British aid in the event of war, but Grey persuaded him the talks must continue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins242–244_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins242–244-176">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More public was the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-German_naval_arms_race" title="Anglo-German naval arms race">naval arms race</a> between Britain and Germany. The Moroccan crisis had been settled at the <a href="/wiki/Algeciras_Conference" title="Algeciras Conference">Algeciras Conference</a>, and Campbell-Bannerman's cabinet approved reduced naval estimates, including postponing the laying down of a second <a href="/wiki/HMS_Dreadnought_(1906)" title="HMS Dreadnought (1906)"><i>Dreadnought</i>-class battleship</a>. Tenser relationships with Germany, and that nation moving ahead with its own <a href="/wiki/Dreadnoughts" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreadnoughts">dreadnoughts</a>, led <a href="/wiki/Reginald_McKenna" title="Reginald McKenna">Reginald McKenna</a>, when Asquith appointed him <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Admiralty" title="First Lord of the Admiralty">First Lord of the Admiralty</a> in 1908, to propose the laying down of eight more British ones in the following three years. This prompted conflict in the Cabinet between those who supported this programme, such as McKenna, and the "economists" who promoted economy in naval estimates, led by Lloyd George and Churchill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley474–475_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley474–475-177">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> There was much public sentiment for building as many ships as possible to maintain British naval superiority. Asquith mediated among his colleagues and secured a compromise whereby four ships would be laid down at once, and four more if there proved to be a need.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss108–109_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss108–109-178">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The armaments matter was put to the side during the domestic crises over the 1909 budget and then the Parliament Act, though the building of warships continued at an accelerated rate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst518–519_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst518–519-179">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Agadir_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Agadir crisis">Agadir crisis</a> of 1911 was again between France and Germany over Moroccan interests, but Asquith's government signalled its friendliness towards France in Lloyd George's <a href="/wiki/Mansion_House_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Mansion House speech">Mansion House speech</a> on 21 July.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulligan71_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulligan71-180">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Late that year, the Lord President of the Council, <a href="/wiki/John_Morley" title="John Morley">Viscount Morley</a>, brought the question of the communications with the French to the attention of the Cabinet. The Cabinet agreed (at Asquith's instigation) that no talks could be held that committed Britain to war, and required cabinet approval for co-ordinated military actions. Nevertheless, by 1912, the French had requested additional naval co-ordination and late in the year, the various understandings were committed to writing in an exchange of letters between Grey and French Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cambon" title="Paul Cambon">Paul Cambon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins242–245_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins242–245-181">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The relationship with France disquieted some Liberal backbenchers and Asquith felt obliged to assure them that nothing had been secretly agreed that would commit Britain to war. This quieted Asquith's foreign policy critics until another naval estimates dispute erupted early in 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst519_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst519-182">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="July_Crisis_and_outbreak_of_World_War_I">July Crisis and outbreak of World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: July Crisis and outbreak of World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_World_War_I" title="Causes of World War I">Causes of World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/July_Crisis" title="July Crisis">July Crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Portrait_of_Edward_Grey%2C_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg/220px-Portrait_of_Edward_Grey%2C_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Portrait_of_Edward_Grey%2C_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg/330px-Portrait_of_Edward_Grey%2C_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Portrait_of_Edward_Grey%2C_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg/440px-Portrait_of_Edward_Grey%2C_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1067" data-file-height="1267" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon" title="Edward Grey, 1st Viscount Grey of Fallodon">Sir Edward Grey</a></div></div></div>
<p>The assassination of <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand_of_Austria" title="Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria">Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> on 28 June 1914 initiated a month of unsuccessful diplomatic attempts to avoid war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar11_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar11-183">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> These attempts ended with Grey's proposal for a four-power conference of Britain, Germany, France and Italy, following the Austrian ultimatum to <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> on the evening of 23 July. Grey's initiative was rejected by Germany as "not practicable".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199523_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199523-184">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> During this period, George Cassar considers that; "The country was overwhelmingly opposed to intervention."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar19_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar19-185">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Much of Asquith's cabinet was similarly inclined, Lloyd George told a journalist on 27 July that "there could be no question of our taking part in any war in the first instance. He knew of no Minister who would be in favour of it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199523_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199523-184">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and wrote in his <i>War Memoirs</i> that before the German ultimatum to Belgium on 3 August "The Cabinet was hopelessly divided—fully one third, if not one half, being opposed to our entry into the War. After the German ultimatum to Belgium the Cabinet was almost unanimous."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd_George_Volume_I66_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd_George_Volume_I66-186">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>  Asquith himself, while growing more aware of the impending catastrophe, was still uncertain of the necessity for Britain's involvement. On 24 July, he wrote to Venetia; "We are within measurable, or imaginable, distance of a real <a href="/wiki/Armageddon" title="Armageddon">Armageddon</a>. Happily there seems to be no reason why we should be anything more than spectators."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985123_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985123-187">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the continuing escalation Asquith "used all his experience and authority to keep his options open"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar20_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar20-188">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> and adamantly refused to commit his government; "The worst thing we could do would be to announce to the world at the present moment that in <i>no circumstances</i> would we intervene."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985133_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985133-189">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> But he recognised Grey's clear commitment to Anglo-French unity and, following Russian mobilisation on 30 July,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199527_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199527-190">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Kaiser's</a> ultimatum to the <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicholas II">Tsar</a> on 1 August, he recognised the inevitability of war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar15_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar15-191">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> From this point, he committed himself to participation, despite continuing Cabinet opposition; "There is a strong party reinforced by Ll George[,] Morley and Harcourt who are against any kind of intervention. Grey will never consent and I shall not separate myself from him."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985146_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985146-192">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Also, on 2 August, he received confirmation of Tory support from Bonar Law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss159_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss159-193">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> In one of two extraordinary Cabinets held on that Sunday, Grey informed members of the 1912 Anglo-French naval talks and Asquith secured agreement to mobilise the fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastings88_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastings88-194">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On Monday 3 August, the Belgian Government rejected the German demand for free passage through its country and in the afternoon, "with gravity and unexpected eloquence",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss159_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss159-193">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Grey spoke in the Commons and called for British action "against the unmeasured aggrandisement of any power".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastings93_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastings93-195">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Basil_Liddell_Hart" class="mw-redirect" title="Basil Liddell Hart">Basil Liddell Hart</a> considered that this speech saw the "hardening (of) British opinion to the point of intervention".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart50_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart50-196">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The following day Asquith saw the King and an ultimatum to Germany demanding withdrawal from Belgian soil was issued with a deadline of midnight Berlin time, 11.00 p.m. (<a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">GMT</a>). Margot Asquith described the moment of expiry, somewhat inaccurately; "(I joined) Henry in the Cabinet room. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Crewe-Milnes,_1st_Marquess_of_Crewe" title="Robert Crewe-Milnes, 1st Marquess of Crewe">Lord Crewe</a> and Sir Edward Grey were already there and we sat smoking cigarettes in silence&#160;… The clock on the mantelpiece hammered out the hour and when the last beat of midnight struck it was as silent as dawn. We were at War."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962294–295_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962294–295-197">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="First_year_of_the_war:_August_1914_.E2.80.93_May_1915"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_year_of_the_war:_August_1914_–_May_1915">First year of the war: August 1914 – May 1915</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: First year of the war: August 1914 – May 1915">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the United Kingdom during World War I">History of the United Kingdom during World War I</a></div>
<h3><span id="Asquith.27s_wartime_government"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Asquith's_wartime_government">Asquith's wartime government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Asquith&#039;s wartime government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The declaration of war on 4 August 1914 saw Asquith as the head of an almost united Liberal Party. Having persuaded <a href="/wiki/John_Simon,_1st_Viscount_Simon" title="John Simon, 1st Viscount Simon">Sir John Simon</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Lygon,_7th_Earl_Beauchamp" title="William Lygon, 7th Earl Beauchamp">Lord Beauchamp</a> to remain,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b10_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b10-198">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Asquith suffered only two resignations from his cabinet, those of <a href="/wiki/John_Morley" title="John Morley">John Morley</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Burns" title="John Burns">John Burns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobhouse180_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobhouse180-199">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> With other parties promising to co-operate, Asquith's government declared war on behalf of a united nation, Asquith bringing "the country into war without civil disturbance or political schism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar234_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar234-200">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first months of the War saw a revival in Asquith's popularity. Bitterness from earlier struggles temporarily receded and the nation looked to Asquith, "steady, massive, self-reliant and unswerving",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar31_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar31-201">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> to lead them to victory. But Asquith's peacetime strengths ill-equipped him for what was to become perhaps the first <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> and, before its end, he would be out of office forever and his party would never again form a majority government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar232_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar232-202">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond the replacement of Morley and Burns,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1923220–221_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1923220–221-203">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Asquith made one other significant change to his cabinet. He relinquished the War Office and appointed the non-partisan but Tory-inclined <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">Lord Kitchener of Khartoum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar38_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar38-204">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Kitchener was a figure of national renown and his participation strengthened the reputation of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1923219_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1923219-205">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Whether it increased its effectiveness is less certain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdelman11_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdelman11-107">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Overall, it was a government of considerable talent with Lloyd George remaining as chancellor,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar37_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar37-206">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Grey as Foreign Secretary,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar36_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar36-207">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> and Churchill at the Admiralty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar38_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar38-204">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The invasion of Belgium by German forces, the touch paper for British intervention, saw the Kaiser's armies attempt a lightning strike through Belgium against France, while holding Russian forces on the Eastern Front.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart69_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart69-208">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> To support the French, Asquith's cabinet authorised the despatch of the <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_I)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War I)">British Expeditionary Force</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199537_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_199537-209">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> The ensuing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Frontiers" title="Battle of the Frontiers">Battle of the Frontiers</a> in the late summer and early autumn of 1914 saw the final halt of the German advance at the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="First Battle of the Marne">First Battle of the Marne</a>, which established the pattern of <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attritional trench warfare</a> on the Western Front that continued until 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart131_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart131-210">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> This stalemate brought deepening resentment against the government, and against Asquith personally, as the population at large and the press lords in particular, blamed him for a lack of energy in the prosecution of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar93_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar93-211">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> It also created divisions within the Cabinet between the "Westerners", including Asquith, who supported the generals in believing that the key to victory lay in ever greater investment of men and munitions in France and Belgium,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar171_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar171-212">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> and the "Easterners", led by Churchill and Lloyd George, who believed that the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> was in a state of irreversible stasis and sought victory through action in the East.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar144_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar144-213">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Lastly, it highlighted divisions between those politicians, and newspaper owners, who thought that military strategy and actions should be determined by the generals, and those who thought politicians should make those decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor109_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor109-214">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Asquith said in his memoirs: "Once the governing objectives have been decided by Ministers at home—the execution should always be left to the untrammeled discretion of the commanders on the spot."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928a154_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928a154-215">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Lloyd George's counter view was expressed in a letter of early 1916 in which he asked "whether I have a right to express an independent view on the War or must (be) a pure advocate of opinions expressed by my military advisers?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985390_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985390-216">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> These divergent opinions lay behind the two great crises that would, within 14 months, see the collapse of the last ever fully <a href="/wiki/First_Asquith_ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="First Asquith ministry">Liberal administration</a> and the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Asquith_coalition_ministry" title="Asquith coalition ministry">first coalition</a>, the Dardanelles Campaign and the Shell Crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford273–274_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford273–274-217">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dardanelles_Campaign">Dardanelles Campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Dardanelles Campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli Campaign</a></div> 
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adm._John_Fisher.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Adm._John_Fisher.jpg/170px-Adm._John_Fisher.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Adm._John_Fisher.jpg/255px-Adm._John_Fisher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Adm._John_Fisher.jpg/340px-Adm._John_Fisher.jpg 2x" data-file-width="677" data-file-height="951" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adm._John_Fisher.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">Admiral "Jacky" Fisher</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Dardanelles Campaign</a> was an attempt by Churchill and those favouring an Eastern strategy to end the stalemate on the Western Front. It envisaged an Anglo-French landing on Turkey's Gallipoli Peninsula and a rapid advance to Constantinople which would see the exit of Turkey from the conflict. The plan was rejected by <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">Admiral Fisher</a>, the <a href="/wiki/First_Sea_Lord" title="First Sea Lord">First Sea Lord</a>, and Kitchener.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Unable to provide decisive leadership, Asquith sought to arbitrate between these two and Churchill, leading to procrastination and delay. The naval attempt was badly defeated. Allied troops established bridgeheads on the Gallipoli Peninsula, but a delay in providing sufficient reinforcements allowed the Turks to regroup, leading to a stalemate Jenkins described "as immobile as that which prevailed on the Western Front".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins354_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins354-219">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> The Allies suffered from infighting at the top, poor equipment, incompetent leadership, and lack of planning, while facing the best units of the Ottoman army. The Allies sent in 492,000 men; they suffered 132,000 casualties (mostly from Australian and New Zealand units) in the humiliating defeat. Politically, it ruined Churchill and badly hurt Asquith.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shell_Crisis_of_May_1915">Shell Crisis of May 1915</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Shell Crisis of May 1915">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">Shell Crisis of 1915</a></div>
<p>The opening of 1915 saw growing division between Lloyd George and Kitchener over the supply of munitions for the army. Lloyd George considered that a munitions department, under his control, was essential to co-ordinate "the nation's entire engineering capacity".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar84_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar84-221">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Kitchener favoured the continuance of the current arrangement whereby munitions were sourced through contracts between the War Office and the country's armaments manufacturers. As so often, Asquith sought compromise through committee, establishing a group to "consider the much vexed question of putting the contracts for munitions on a proper footing".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985497_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985497-222">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> This did little to dampen press criticism<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar87_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar87-223">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> and, on 20 April, Asquith sought to challenge his detractors in a major speech at Newcastle; "I saw a statement the other day that the operations of our army were being crippled by our failure to provide the necessary ammunition. There is not a word of truth in that statement."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar88_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar88-224">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The press response was savage: 14 May 1915 saw the publication in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> of a letter from their correspondent <a href="/wiki/Charles_%C3%A0_Court_Repington" title="Charles à Court Repington">Charles à Court Repington</a> which ascribed the British failure at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aubers_Ridge" title="Battle of Aubers Ridge">Battle of Aubers Ridge</a> to a shortage of high explosive shells. Thus opened a fully-fledged crisis, <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">the Shell Crisis</a>. The prime minister's wife correctly identified her husband's chief opponent, the Press baron, and owner of <i>The Times</i>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Northcliffe" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Northcliffe">Lord Northcliffe</a>; "I'm quite sure Northcliffe is at the bottom of all this,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014128_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014128-225">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> but failed to recognise the clandestine involvement of <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John French">Sir John French</a>, who leaked the details of the shells shortage to Repington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell111_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell111-226">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Northcliffe claimed that "the whole question of the supply of the munitions of war is one on which the Cabinet cannot be arraigned too sharply."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson237_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson237-227">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Attacks on the government and on Asquith's personal lethargy came from the left as well as the right, <a href="/wiki/C._P._Scott" title="C. P. Scott">C. P. Scott</a>, the editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Manchester Guardian</a></i> writing; "The Government has failed most frightfully and discreditably in the matter of munitions."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott124_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott124-228">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_events">Other events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Other events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Failures in both the East and the West began a tide of events that was to overwhelm Asquith's Liberal Government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins355_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins355-229">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Strategic setbacks combined with a shattering personal blow when, on 12 May 1915, Venetia Stanley announced her engagement to <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Samuel_Montagu" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin Samuel Montagu">Edwin Montagu</a>. Asquith's reply was immediate and brief, "As you know well, this breaks my heart. I couldn't bear to come and see you. I can only pray God to bless you—and help me."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985593_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985593-230">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Venetia's importance to him is illustrated by a letter written in mid-1914; "Keep close to me beloved in this most critical time of my life. I know you will not fail."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985101_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1985101-231">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Her engagement; "a very treacherous return after all the joy you've given me", left him devastated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford271_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford271-232">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Significant though the loss was personally, its impact on Asquith politically can be overstated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss186_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss186-233">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> The historian Stephen Koss notes that Asquith "was always able to divide his public and private lives into separate compartments (and) soon found new confidantes to whom he was writing with no less frequency, ardour and indiscretion."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss186–187_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss186–187-234">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This personal loss was immediately followed, on 15 May, by the resignation of Admiral Fisher after continuing disagreements with Churchill and in frustration at the disappointing developments in Gallipoli.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell112_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell112-235">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Aged 74, Fisher's behaviour had grown increasingly erratic and, in frequent letters to Lloyd George, he gave vent to his frustrations with the <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Admiralty" title="First Lord of the Admiralty">First Lord of the Admiralty</a>; "Fisher writes to me every day or two to let me know how things are going. He has a great deal of trouble with his chief, who is always wanting to do something big and striking."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToye136_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToye136-236">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Adverse events, press hostility, Tory opposition and personal sorrows assailed Asquith, and his position was further weakened by his Liberal colleagues. Cassar considers that Lloyd George displayed a distinct lack of loyalty,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar100_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar100-237">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> and Koss writes of the contemporary rumours that Churchill had "been up to his old game of intriguing all round" and reports a claim that Churchill "unquestionably inspired" the Repington Letter, in collusion with Sir John French.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss193_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss193-238">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Lacking cohesion internally, and attacked from without, Asquith determined that his government could not continue and he wrote to the King, "I have come decidedly to the conclusion that the [Government] must be reconstituted on a broad and non-party basis."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins360_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins360-239">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="First_Coalition:_May_1915_.E2.80.93_December_1916"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Coalition:_May_1915_–_December_1916">First Coalition: May 1915 – December 1916</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: First Coalition: May 1915 – December 1916">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Asquith_coalition_ministry" title="Asquith coalition ministry">Asquith coalition ministry</a></div>
