<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg/170px-Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg/255px-Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg/340px-Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="596" data-file-height="770" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Bonar_Law_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a></div></div></div>
<p>The formation of the First Coalition saw Asquith display the political acuteness that seemed to have deserted him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985254_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985254-240">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> But it came at a cost. This involved the sacrifice of two old political comrades: Churchill, who was blamed for the Dardanelles fiasco, and Haldane, who was wrongly accused in the press of pro-German sympathies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins360_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins360-239">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> The Tories under <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> made these removals a condition of entering government and, in sacking Haldane, who "made no difficulty", <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaldane286_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaldane286-241">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Asquith, committed "the most uncharacteristic fault of (his) whole career".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins362_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins362-242">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> In a letter to Grey, Asquith wrote of Haldane; "He is the oldest personal and political friend that I have in the world and, with him, you and I have stood together for the best part of 30 years."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrevelyan278_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrevelyan278-243">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> But he was unable to express these sentiments directly to Haldane, who was greatly hurt. Asquith handled the allocation of offices more successfully, appointing Bonar Law to the relatively minor post of Colonial Secretary,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams188_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams188-244">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> taking responsibility for munitions from Kitchener and giving it, as a new ministry, to Lloyd George and placing Balfour at the Admiralty, in place of Churchill, who was demoted to the sinecure Cabinet post of <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Duchy_of_Lancaster" title="Chancellor of the Duchy of Lancaster">Chancellor of the Duchy of Lancaster</a>. Overall the Liberals held 12 Cabinet seats, including most of the important ones, while the Tories held 8.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams193_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams193-245">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Despite this outcome, many Liberals were dismayed, the sacked <a href="/wiki/Charles_Hobhouse" title="Charles Hobhouse">Charles Hobhouse</a> writing; "The disintegration of the Liberal Party is complete. Ll.G. and his Tory friends will soon get rid of Asquith."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobhouse247_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobhouse247-246">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> From a party, and a personal, perspective, the creation of the First Coalition was seen as a "notable victory for (Asquith), if not for the allied cause".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985254_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985254-240">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> But Asquith's dismissive handling of Bonar Law also contributed to his own and his party's later destruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard68_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard68-247">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_re-organisation">War re-organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: War re-organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Having reconstructed his government, Asquith attempted a re-configuration of his war-making apparatus. The most important element of this was the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Munitions" title="Minister of Munitions">Ministry of Munitions</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins368_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins368-248">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> followed by the re-ordering of the War Council into a Dardanelles Committee, with <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Hankey,_1st_Baron_Hankey" title="Maurice Hankey, 1st Baron Hankey">Maurice Hankey</a> as secretary and with a remit to consider all questions of war strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins370_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins370-249">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Munitions_of_War_Act_1915" title="Munitions of War Act 1915">Munitions of War Act 1915</a> brought private companies supplying the armed forces under the tight control of the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Munitions" title="Minister of Munitions">Minister of Munitions</a>, Lloyd George. The policy, according to <a href="/wiki/J._A._R._Marriott" class="mw-redirect" title="J. A. R. Marriott">J. A. R. Marriott</a>, was that:
</p>
<dl><dd>no private interest was to be permitted to obstruct the service, or imperil the safety, of the State. Trade Union regulations must be suspended; employers' profits must be limited, skilled men must fight, if not in the trenches, in the factories; man-power must be economised by the dilution of labour and the employment of women; Private factories must pass under the control of the State, and new national factories be set up. Results justified the new policy: the output was prodigious; the goods were at last delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Nevertheless, criticism of Asquith's leadership style continued. <a href="/wiki/David_Lindsay,_27th_Earl_of_Crawford" title="David Lindsay, 27th Earl of Crawford">The Earl of Crawford</a>, who had joined the Government as Minister of Agriculture, described his first Cabinet meeting; "It was a huge gathering, so big that it is hopeless for more than one or two to express opinions on each detail […] Asquith somnolent—hands shaky and cheeks pendulous. He exercised little control over debate, seemed rather bored, but good humoured throughout." Lloyd George was less tolerant, Lord Riddell recording in his diary; "(He) says the P.M. should <b>lead</b> not follow and (Asquith) never moves until he is forced, and then it is usually too late."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell147_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell147-251">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> And crises, as well as criticism, continued to assail the Prime Minister, "envenomed by intra-party as well as inter-party rancour".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985308_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985308-252">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conscription">Conscription</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Conscription">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recruitment_to_the_British_Army_during_the_First_World_War" title="Recruitment to the British Army during the First World War">Recruitment to the British Army during the First World War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg/220px-Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg/330px-Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg/440px-Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2401" data-file-height="3546" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Another_call_%22More_men_and_still_more_until_the_enemy_is_crushed%22_Lord_Kitchener_LCCN2003662914-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lord Kitchener's call to arms</div></div></div>
<p>The insatiable demand for manpower for the Western Front had been foreseen early on. A volunteer system had been introduced at the outbreak of war, and Asquith was reluctant to change it for political reasons, as many Liberals, and almost all of their Irish Nationalist and Labour allies, were strongly opposed to <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Conscription in the United Kingdom">conscription</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins371_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins371-253">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Volunteer numbers dropped,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar151_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar151-254">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> not meeting the demands for more troops for Gallipoli, and much more strongly, for the Western Front.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins373_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins373-255">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> This made the voluntary system increasingly untenable; Asquith's daughter Violet wrote in March 1915; "Gradually every man with the average number of limbs and faculties is being sucked out to the war."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter33_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter33-256">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> In July 1915, the National Registration Act was passed, requiring compulsory registration for all men between the ages of 18 and 65.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar150_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar150-257">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> This was seen by many as the prelude to conscription but the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Stanley,_17th_Earl_of_Derby" title="Edward Stanley, 17th Earl of Derby">Lord Derby</a> as Director-General of Recruiting instead saw an attempt to rejuvenate the voluntary system, the <a href="/wiki/Derby_Scheme" title="Derby Scheme">Derby Scheme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToye155_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToye155-258">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's slow steps towards conscription continued to infuriate his opponents, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Wilson,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Henry Wilson, 1st Baronet">Sir Henry Wilson</a> writing to <a href="/wiki/Leo_Amery" title="Leo Amery">Leo Amery</a>; "What is going to be the result of these debates? Will 'wait and see' win, or can that part of the Cabinet that is in earnest and is honest force that damned old Squiff into action?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmery124_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmery124-259">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> The Prime Minister's balancing act, within Parliament and within his own party, was not assisted by a strident campaign against conscription conducted by his wife. Describing herself as "passionately against it",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014180_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014180-260">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Margot Asquith engaged in one of her frequent influencing drives, by letters and through conversations, which had little impact other than doing "great harm" to Asquith's reputation and position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014175_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014175-261">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of 1915, it was clear that conscription was essential and Asquith laid the <a href="/wiki/Military_Service_Act_1916" title="Military Service Act 1916">Military Service Act</a> in the House of Commons on 5 January 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar162_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar162-262">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> The Act introduced conscription of bachelors, and was extended to married men later in the year. Asquith's main opposition came from within his own party, particularly from Sir John Simon, who resigned. Asquith described Simon's stance in a letter to Sylvia Henley; "I felt really like a man who had been struck publicly in the face by his son."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar163_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar163-263">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Some years later, Simon acknowledged his error; "I have long since realised that my opposition was a mistake."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon107_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon107-264">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's achievement in bringing the bill through without breaking up the government was considerable, his wife writing; "Henry's patience and skill in keeping Labour in this amazing change in England have stunned everyone,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014257_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014257-265">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> but the long struggle "hurt his own reputation and the unity of his party".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar169_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar169-266">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a></div>
<p>On Easter Monday 1916, a group of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Volunteers" title="Irish Volunteers">Irish Volunteers</a> and members of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Citizen_Army" title="Irish Citizen Army">Irish Citizen Army</a> seized a number of key buildings and locations in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> and elsewhere. There was heavy fighting over the next week before the Volunteers were forced to surrender.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins395_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins395-267">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Distracted by conscription, Asquith and the Government were slow to appreciate the developing danger, <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985348_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985348-268">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> which was exacerbated when, after hasty courts martial, a number of the Irish leaders were executed. On 11 May Asquith crossed to Dublin and, after a week of investigation, decided that the island's governance system was irredeemably broken,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins398_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins398-269">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> He turned to Lloyd George for a solution. With his customary energy, Lloyd George brokered a settlement which would have seen <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_movement" title="Irish Home Rule movement">Home Rule</a> introduced at the end of the War, with the exclusion of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985351_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985351-270">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> However, neither he, nor Asquith, appreciated the extent of Tory opposition, the plan was strongly attacked in the House of Lords, and was abandoned thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985352_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985352-271">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> The episode damaged Lloyd George's reputation, but also that of Asquith, Walter Long speaking of the latter as; "terribly lacking in decision".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell166_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell166-272">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> It also further widened the divide between Asquith and Lloyd George, and encouraged the latter in his plans for government reconstruction; "Mr. A gets very few cheers nowadays."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell167_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell167-273">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Progress_of_the_war">Progress of the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Progress of the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_the_Somme,_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_Battle_of_the_Somme%2C_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg/220px-The_Battle_of_the_Somme%2C_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_Battle_of_the_Somme%2C_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg/330px-The_Battle_of_the_Somme%2C_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_Battle_of_the_Somme%2C_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg/440px-The_Battle_of_the_Somme%2C_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="635" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_the_Somme,_July-november_1916_Q4192.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith visits the front during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a>, 1916</div></div></div>
<p>Continued Allied failure and heavy losses at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Loos" title="Battle of Loos">Battle of Loos</a> between September and October 1915 ended any remaining confidence in the British commander, <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John French">Sir John French</a> and in the judgement of Lord Kitchener.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins380_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins380-274">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Asquith resorted to a favoured stratagem and, persuading Kitchener to undertake a tour of the Gallipoli battlefield in the hope that he could be persuaded to remain in the Mediterranean as Commander-in-Chief,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_1971562_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_1971562-275">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> took temporary charge of the War Office himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins381_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins381-276">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> He then replaced French with <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Haig,_1st_Earl_Haig" title="Douglas Haig, 1st Earl Haig">Sir Douglas Haig</a>; the latter recording in his diary for 10 December 1915; "About 7&#160;pm I received a letter from the Prime Minister marked 'Secret' and enclosed in <i>three</i> envelopes. It ran 'Sir J. French has placed in my hands his resignation&#160;… Subject to the King's approval, I have the pleasure of proposing to you that you should be his successor.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield_&amp;_Bourne172_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield_&amp;_Bourne172-277">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> Asquith also appointed <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Robertson,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir William Robertson, 1st Baronet">Sir William Robertson</a> as <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Imperial_General_Staff" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief of the Imperial General Staff">Chief of the Imperial General Staff</a> with increased powers, reporting directly to the Cabinet and with the sole right to give them military advice, relegating the Secretary of State for War to the tasks of recruiting and supplying the army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar136_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar136-278">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Lastly, he instituted a smaller Dardanelles Committee, re-christened the War Committee,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToye156_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToye156-279">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> with himself, Balfour, Bonar Law, Lloyd George and Reginald McKenna as members<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_19721249_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_19721249-280">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> although, as this soon increased, the Committee continued the failings of its predecessor, being "too large and lack(ing) executive authority".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar134_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar134-281">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> None of this saved the Dardanelles Campaign and the decision to evacuate was taken in December,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985325_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985325-282">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> resulting in the resignation from the Duchy of Lancaster of Churchill,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_1971564_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_1971564-283">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> who wrote, "I could not accept a position of general responsibility for war policy without any effective share in its guidance and control."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_19721249_280-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_19721249-280">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Further reverses took place in the Balkans: the Central Powers overran Serbia, forcing the Allied troops which had attempted to intervene back towards <a href="/wiki/Salonika_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Salonika Campaign">Salonika</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985322–324_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985322–324-284">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early 1916 saw the start of the German offensive at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun" title="Battle of Verdun">Verdun</a>, the "greatest battle of attrition in history".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert_1995231_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert_1995231-285">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> In late May, the only significant Anglo-German naval engagement of the War took place at <a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Jutland" class="mw-redirect" title="The Battle of Jutland">The Battle of Jutland</a>. Although a strategic success,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar185_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar185-286">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> the greater loss of ships on the Allied side brought early dismay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985342_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985342-287">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Legh,_2nd_Baron_Newton" title="Thomas Legh, 2nd Baron Newton">Lord Newton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paymaster_General" title="Paymaster General">Paymaster General</a> and Parliamentary spokesman for the War office in Kitchener's absence, recorded in his diary; "Stupefying news of naval battle off Jutland. Whilst listening to the list of ships lost, I thought it the worst disaster that we had ever suffered."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewton222_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewton222-288">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> This despondency was compounded, for the nation, if not for his colleagues, when Lord Kitchener was killed in the sinking of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hampshire_(1903)" title="HMS Hampshire (1903)">HMS <i>Hampshire</i></a> on 5 June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins405_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins405-289">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith first considered taking the vacant War Office himself but then offered it to Bonar Law, who declined it in favour of Lloyd George.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins406–407_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins406–407-290">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> This was an important sign of growing unity of action between the two men and it filled Margot Asquith with foreboding; "I look upon this as the greatest political blunder of Henry's lifetime, … We are out: it can only be a question of time now when we shall have to leave Downing Street."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014268_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014268-291">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985360_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985360-292">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raymond_Asquith02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Raymond_Asquith02.jpg/150px-Raymond_Asquith02.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Raymond_Asquith02.jpg/225px-Raymond_Asquith02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Raymond_Asquith02.jpg/300px-Raymond_Asquith02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="316" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raymond_Asquith02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Raymond_Asquith" title="Raymond Asquith">Raymond Asquith</a></div></div></div>
<p>Asquith followed this by agreeing to hold Commissions of Inquiry into the conduct of the Dardanelles and of the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_campaign" title="Mesopotamian campaign">Mesopotamian campaign</a>, where Allied forces had been forced to surrender at <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kut" title="Siege of Kut">Kut</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins410_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins410-293">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Hankey,_1st_Baron_Hankey" title="Maurice Hankey, 1st Baron Hankey">Sir Maurice Hankey</a>, Secretary to the War Committee, considered that; "the Coalition never recovered. For (its) last five months, the function of the Supreme Command was carried out under the shadow of these inquests."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins411_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins411-294">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> But these mistakes were overshadowed by the limited progress and immense casualties of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a>, which began on 1 July 1916, and then by another devastating personal loss, the death of Asquith's son <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Asquith" title="Raymond Asquith">Raymond</a>, on 15 September at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flers%E2%80%93Courcelette" title="Battle of Flers–Courcelette">Battle of Flers–Courcelette</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford367_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford367-295">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's relationship with his eldest son had not been easy. Raymond wrote to his wife in early 1916; "If Margot talks any more bosh to you about the inhumanity of her stepchildren you can stop her mouth by telling her that during my 10 months exile here the P.M. has never written me a line of any description."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond_Asquith286–287_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond_Asquith286–287-296">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> But Raymond's death was shattering, Violet writing; "…to see Father suffering so wrings one",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter95_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter95-297">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> and Asquith passed much of the following months "withdrawn and difficult to approach".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins415_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins415-298">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> The War brought no respite, Churchill writing that; "The failure to break the German line in the Somme, the recovery of the Germanic powers in the East [i.e. the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Brusilov_Offensive" title="Brusilov Offensive">Brusilov Offensive</a>], the <a href="/wiki/Romania_during_World_War_I" title="Romania during World War I">ruin of Roumania</a> and the beginnings of renewed submarine warfare strengthened and stimulated all those forces which insisted upon still greater vigour in the conduct of affairs."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChurchill1139_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChurchill1139-299">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Fall:_November.E2.80.93December_1916"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall:_November–December_1916">Fall: November–December 1916</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Fall: November–December 1916">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The events that led to the collapse of the First Coalition were exhaustively chronicled by almost all of the major participants,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss217_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss217-300">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> (although Asquith himself was a notable exception), and have been minutely studied by historians in the 100 years since.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar211_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar211-301">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Although many of the accounts and studies differ in detail, and present a somewhat confusing picture overall, the outline is clear. As <a href="/wiki/R._J._Q._Adams" title="R. J. Q. Adams">R. J. Q. Adams</a> wrote; "The Prime Minister depended upon [a] majority [in] Parliament. The faith of that majority in Asquith's leadership had been shaken and the appearance of a logical alternative destroyed him."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams243_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams243-302">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Nigeria_debate_and_Lord_Lansdowne.27s_memorandum"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nigeria_debate_and_Lord_Lansdowne's_memorandum">Nigeria debate and Lord Lansdowne's memorandum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Nigeria debate and Lord Lansdowne&#039;s memorandum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Aitken,_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Max_Aitken%2C_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg/220px-Max_Aitken%2C_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="348" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Max_Aitken%2C_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg/330px-Max_Aitken%2C_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Max_Aitken%2C_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg/440px-Max_Aitken%2C_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1712" data-file-height="2708" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Aitken,_Lord_Beaverbrook.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"a man called Max Aitken"</div></div></div>
<p>The touch paper for the final crisis was the unlikely subject of the sale of captured German assets in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams222_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams222-305">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> As Colonial Secretary, the Conservative leader Bonar Law led the debate and was subject to a furious attack by Sir Edward Carson. The issue itself was trivial,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss214_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss214-306">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> but the fact that Law had been attacked by a leading member of his own party, and was not supported by Lloyd George (who absented himself from the House only to dine with Carson later in the evening), was not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss215_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss215-307">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Margot Asquith immediately sensed the coming danger; "From that night it was quite clear that Northcliffe, Rothermere, Bonar, Carson, Ll.G (and a man called Max Aitken) were going to run the Government. I knew it was the end."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014306_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014306-308">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> Grey was similarly prescient, writing; "Lloyd George means to break up the Government."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrey248_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrey248-309">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> Bonar Law saw the debate as a threat to his own political position,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBridgeman111–112_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBridgeman111–112-310">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> as well as another instance of lack of grip by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams223_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams223-311">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The situation was further inflamed by the publication of a memorandum on future prospects in the war by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Petty-Fitzmaurice,_5th_Marquess_of_Lansdowne" title="Henry Petty-Fitzmaurice, 5th Marquess of Lansdowne">Lord Lansdowne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins418_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins418-312">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Circulated on 13 November, it considered, and did not dismiss, the possibility of a negotiated settlement with the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>. Asquith's critics immediately assumed that the memorandum represented his own views and that Lansdowne was being used as a stalking horse,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins419_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins419-313">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Crewe-Milnes,_1st_Marquess_of_Crewe" title="Robert Crewe-Milnes, 1st Marquess of Crewe">Lord Crewe</a> going so far as to suggest that the Lansdowne Memorandum was the "veritable <i>causa causans</i><sup id="cite_ref-causa_causans_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-causa_causans-314">&#91;n&#93;</a></sup> of the final break-up".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPope-Hennessy181_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPope-Hennessy181-315">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Triumvirate_gathers">Triumvirate gathers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Triumvirate gathers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 20 November 1916 Lloyd George, Carson and Bonar Law met at the <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Oriental_Hyde_Park,_London" title="Mandarin Oriental Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park Hotel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar212_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar212-316">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> The meeting was organised by <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Max Aitken</a> who was to play central roles both in the forthcoming crisis and in its subsequent historiography.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell181_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell181-317">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Max Aitken was a Canadian adventurer, millionaire, and close friend of Bonar Law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams224_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams224-318">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> His book on the fall of the First Coalition, <i>Politicians and the War 1914–1916</i>, although always partial and sometimes inaccurate, gives a detailed insider's view of the events leading up to Asquith's political demise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor121_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor121-319">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> The trio agreed on the necessity of overhauling the government and further agreed on the mechanism for doing so; the establishment of a small War Council, chaired by Lloyd George, with no more than five members and with full executive authority for the conduct of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott243_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott243-320">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith was to be retained as prime minister, and given honorific oversight of the War Council, but day to day operations would be directed by Lloyd George.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar212_316-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar212-316">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> This scheme, although often reworked, remained the basis of all proposals to reform the government until Asquith's fall on 6 December. Until almost the end, both Bonar Law,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams226_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams226-321">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> and Lloyd George,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985444_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985444-322">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> wished to retain Asquith as premier. But Aitken,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor121_319-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor121-319">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Carson<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor110_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor110-323">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> and Lord Northcliffe emphatically did not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985441_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985441-324">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_without_responsibility">Power without responsibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Power without responsibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Independent_(1849)_(14595849278).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_Independent_%281849%29_%2814595849278%29.jpg/220px-The_Independent_%281849%29_%2814595849278%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_Independent_%281849%29_%2814595849278%29.jpg/330px-The_Independent_%281849%29_%2814595849278%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_Independent_%281849%29_%2814595849278%29.jpg/440px-The_Independent_%281849%29_%2814595849278%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2559" data-file-height="3175" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Independent_(1849)_(14595849278).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lord Northcliffe teeing up</div></div></div>
<p>Lord Northcliffe's role was critical, as was the use Lloyd George made of him, and of the press in general. Northcliffe's involvement also highlights the limitations of both Aitken's and Lloyd George's accounts of Asquith's fall. Both minimised Northcliffe's part in the events. In his <i>War Memoirs</i>, Lloyd George stated emphatically "Lord Northcliffe was never, at any stage, brought into our consultations."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd_George_Volume_II982_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd_George_Volume_II982-325">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> Aitken supported this; "Lord Northcliffe was not in active co-operation with Lloyd George."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook403_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook403-326">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> But these claims are contradicted by others. In their biography of Northcliffe, Reginald Pound and Geoffrey Harmsworth record Northcliffe's brother <a href="/wiki/Harold_Harmsworth,_1st_Viscount_Rothermere" title="Harold Harmsworth, 1st Viscount Rothermere">Rothermere</a> writing contemporaneously; "Alfred has been actively at work with Ll.G. with a view to bringing about a change."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPound_&amp;_Harmsworth513_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPound_&amp;_Harmsworth513-327">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> Riddell wrote in his diary for 27 May 1916: "LG never mentions directly that he sees Northcliffe but I am sure they are in daily contact."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell157_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell157-328">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> Margot Asquith was also certain of Northcliffe's role, and of Lloyd George's involvement, although she obscured both of their names when writing in her diary; "I only hope the man responsible for giving information to Lord N- will be heavily punished: God may forgive him; I never can."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962308_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962308-329">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> They are also contradicted by events; Northcliffe met with Lloyd George on each of the three days just prior to Lloyd George's resignation, on 1, 2, and 3 December,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins445_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins445-330">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> including two meetings on 1 December, both before and after Lloyd George put his revised proposals for the War Council to Asquith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee160_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee160-331">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> It seems improbable that ongoing events were not discussed and that the two men confined their conversations to negotiating article circulation rights for Lloyd George once he had resigned, as Pound and Harmsworth weakly suggest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPound_&amp;_Harmsworth514_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPound_&amp;_Harmsworth514-332">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> The attempts made by others to use Northcliffe and the wider press also merit consideration. In this regard, some senior military officers were extremely active. Robertson, for example, wrote to Northcliffe in October 1916, "The Boche gives me no trouble compared with what I meet in London. So any help you can give me will be of Imperial value."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPound_&amp;_Harmsworth508_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPound_&amp;_Harmsworth508-333">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> Lastly, the actions of Northcliffe's newspapers must be considered—in particular <i>The Times</i> editorial on 4 December which led Asquith to reject Lloyd George's final War Council proposals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins450_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins450-334">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Thompson, Northcliffe's most recent biographer, concludes; "From the evidence, it appears that Northcliffe and his newspapers should be given more credit than they have generally received for the demise of the Asquith government in December 1916."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson264_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson264-335">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="To-ing_and_fro-ing">To-ing and fro-ing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: To-ing and fro-ing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bonar Law met again with Carson and Lloyd George on 25 November and, with Aitken's help, drafted a memorandum for Asquith's signature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams227_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams227-336">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> This would see a "Civilian General Staff", with Lloyd George as chairman and Asquith as president, attending irregularly but with the right of referral to Cabinet as desired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams227_336-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams227-336">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> This, Bonar Law presented to Asquith, who committed to reply on Monday the following week.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar213_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar213-337">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His reply was an outright rejection; the proposal was impossible "without fatally impairing the confidence of colleagues, and undermining my own authority."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar213_337-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar213-337">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> Law took Asquith's response to Carson and Lloyd George at Law's office in the Colonial Office. All were uncertain of the next steps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins426_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins426-338">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Bonar Law decided it would be appropriate to meet with his senior Conservative colleagues, something he had not previously done.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins427_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins427-339">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> He saw <a href="/wiki/Austen_Chamberlain" title="Austen Chamberlain">Austen Chamberlain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Curzon" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Curzon">Lord Curzon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Viscount_Cecil_of_Chelwood" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Viscount Cecil of Chelwood">Lord Robert Cecil</a> on Thursday 30 November. All were united in opposition to Lloyd George's War Council plans, Chamberlain writing; "(we) were unanimously of opinion (sic) that (the plans) were open to grave objection and made certain alternative proposals."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChamberlain117_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChamberlain117-340">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George had also been reflecting on the substance of the scheme and, on Friday 1 December, he met with Asquith to put forward an alternative. This would see a War Council of three, the two Service ministers and a third without portfolio. One of the three, presumably Lloyd George although this was not explicit, would be chairman. Asquith, as Prime Minister, would retain "supreme control."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985450_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985450-341">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's reply the same day did not constitute an outright rejection, but he did demand that he retain the chairmanship of the council.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins430_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins430-342">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> As such, it was unacceptable to Lloyd George and he wrote to Bonar Law the next day (Saturday 2 December); "I enclose copy of P.M.'s letter. The life of the country depends on resolute action by you now."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook406_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook406-343">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_four_days:_Sunday_3_December_to_Wednesday_6_December">Last four days: Sunday 3 December to Wednesday 6 December</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Last four days: Sunday 3 December to Wednesday 6 December">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a four-day crisis Asquith was unaware how fast he was losing support. Lloyd George now had growing Unionist support, the backing of Labour and (thanks to Addison) a majority of Liberal MPs. Asquith fell and Lloyd George answered the loud demands for a much more decisive government. He energetically set up a new small war cabinet, a cabinet secretariat under Hankey, and a secretariat of private advisors in the 'Garden Suburb'; and moved towards prime ministerial control.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunday_3_December">Sunday 3 December</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Sunday 3 December">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sunday 3 December saw the Conservative leadership meet at Bonar Law's house, Pembroke Lodge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook410_345-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook410-345">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> They gathered against a backdrop of ever-growing press involvement, in part fermented by Max Aitken.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor113_346-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor113-346">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> That morning's <i><a href="/wiki/Reynold%27s_News" title="Reynold&#39;s News">Reynold's News</a></i>, owned and edited by Lloyd George's close associate <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dalziel,_1st_Baron_Dalziel_of_Kirkcaldy" title="Henry Dalziel, 1st Baron Dalziel of Kirkcaldy">Henry Dalziel</a>, had published an article setting out Lloyd George's demands to Asquith and claiming that he intended to resign and take his case to the country if they were not met.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook411_347-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook411-347">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> At Law's house, the Conservatives present drew up a resolution which they demanded Law present to Asquith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins435_348-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins435-348">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This document, subsequently the source of much debate, stated that "the Government cannot continue as it is; the Prime Minister (should) tender the resignation of the Government" and, if Asquith was unwilling to do that, the Conservative members of the Government would "tender (their) resignations."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook413_349-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook413-349">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> The meaning of this resolution is unclear, and even those who contributed to it took away differing interpretations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985453_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985453-350">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chamberlain felt that it left open the options of either Asquith or Lloyd George as premier, dependent on who could gain greater support. Curzon, in a letter of that day to Lansdowne, stated that no one at the Pembroke Lodge meeting felt that the war could be won under Asquith's continued leadership, and that the issue for the Liberal politicians to resolve was whether Asquith remained in a Lloyd George administration in a subordinate role, or left the government altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985453–454_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985453–454-351">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> Max Aitken's claim that the resolution's purpose was to ensure that "Lloyd George should go"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook414_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook414-352">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup> is not supported by most of the contemporary accounts,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDutton132–133_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDutton132–133-353">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> or by the assessments of most subsequent historians.
</p><p>As one example, Gilmour, Curzon's biographer, writes that the Unionist ministers "did not, as Beaverbrook alleged, decide to resign themselves in order to strengthen the Prime Minister's hand against Lloyd George..(their intentions) were completely different."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilmour455_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilmour455-354">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, Adams, Bonar Law's latest biographer, describes Aitken's interpretation of the resolution as "convincingly overturned".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams231_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams231-355">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup> Ramsden is equally clear: "the Unionist ministers acted to strengthen Lloyd George's hand, from a conviction that only greater power for Lloyd George could put enough drive into the war effort."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsden132_356-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsden132-356">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bonar Law then took the resolution to Asquith, who had, unusually, broken his weekend at <a href="/wiki/Walmer_Castle" title="Walmer Castle">Walmer Castle</a> to return to Downing Street.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss218_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss218-357">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> At their meeting, Bonar Law sought to convey the content of his colleagues' earlier discussion but failed to produce the resolution itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins439_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins439-358">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> That it was never actually shown to Asquith is incontrovertible, and Asquith confirmed this in his writings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b131_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b131-359">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> Bonar Law's motives in not handing it over are more controversial. Law himself maintained he simply forgot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams232_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams232-360">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> Jenkins charges him with bad faith, or neglect of duty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins440_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins440-361">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> Adams suggests that Law's motives were more complex (the resolution also contained a clause condemning the involvement of the press, prompted by the <i>Reynold's News</i> story of that morning)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook420_362-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook420-362">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> and that, in continuing to seek an accommodation between Asquith and Lloyd George, Law felt it prudent not to share the actual text.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams233_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams233-363">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The outcome of the interview between Law and Asquith was clear, even if Law had not been.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDutton133_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDutton133-364">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> Asquith immediately decided that an accommodation with Lloyd George, and a substantial reconstruction to placate the Unionist ministers, were required.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b132_365-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b132-365">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> He summoned Lloyd George and together they agreed a compromise that was, in fact, little different from Lloyd George's 1 December proposals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins441_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins441-366">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> The only substantial amendment was that Asquith would have daily oversight of the War Council's work and a right of veto.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins441_366-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins441-366">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Grigg_(writer)" title="John Grigg (writer)">John Grigg</a> saw this compromise as "very favourable to Asquith".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985457_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985457-367">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> Cassar is less certain: "The new formula left him in a much weaker position[, his] authority merely on paper for he was unlikely to exercise his veto lest it bring on the collective resignation of the War Council."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar219_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar219-368">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Asquith, Lloyd George, and Bonar Law who had rejoined them at 5.00&#160;pm, all felt a basis for a compromise had been reached, and they agreed that Asquith would issue a bulletin that evening announcing the reconstruction of the Government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar219_368-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar219-368">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> Crewe, who joined Asquith at Montagu's house at 10.00 p.m., recorded: "accommodation with Mr. Lloyd George would ultimately be achieved, without sacrifice of (Asquith's) position as chief of the War Committee; a large measure of reconstruction would satisfy the Unionist Ministers."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPope-Hennessy185_369-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPope-Hennessy185-369">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite Lloyd George's denials of collaboration, the diary for 3 December by Northcliffe's factotum Tom Clarke, records that: "The Chief returned to town and at 7.00 o'clock he was at the War Office with Lloyd George."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith272_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith272-370">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile <a href="/wiki/Duff_Cooper" title="Duff Cooper">Duff Cooper</a> was invited to dinner at Montagu's <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne%27s_Gate" title="Queen Anne&#39;s Gate">Queen Anne's Gate</a> house, he afterwards played bridge with Asquith, Venetia Montagu and Churchill's sister-in-law "Goonie", recording in his diary&#160;: "..the P.M. more drunk than I have ever seen him, (..) so drunk that one felt uncomfortable&#160;… an extraordinary scene."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper40_371-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper40-371">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monday_4_December">Monday 4 December</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Monday 4 December">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The bulletin was published on the morning of Monday 4 December. It was accompanied by an avalanche of press criticism, all of it intensely hostile to Asquith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar221_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar221-372">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> The worst was a leader in Northcliffe's <i>Times</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss219_373-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss219-373">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> This had full details of the compromise reached the day before, including the names of those suggested as members of the War Council. More damagingly still, it ridiculed Asquith, claiming he had conspired in his own humiliation and would henceforth be "Prime Minister in name only."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar221_372-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar221-372">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> Lloyd George's involvement is uncertain; he denied any,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985460_374-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985460-374">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> but Asquith was certain he was the source.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar222_375-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar222-375">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> The author was certainly the editor, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Dawson" title="Geoffrey Dawson">Geoffrey Dawson</a>, with some assistance from Carson. But it seems likely that Carson's source was Lloyd George.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins445_330-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins445-330">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The leak prompted an immediate reaction from Asquith: "Unless the impression is at once corrected that I am being relegated to the position of an irresponsible spectator of the War, I cannot possibly go on."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985460_374-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985460-374">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> Lloyd George's reply was prompt and conciliatory: "I cannot restrain nor I fear influence Northcliffe. I fully accept in letter and in spirit your summary of the suggested arrangement—subject of course to personnel."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins447–448_376-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins447–448-376">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> But Asquith's mind was already turning to rejection of the Sunday compromise and outright confrontation with Lloyd George.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar224_377-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar224-377">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is unclear exactly whom Asquith spoke with on 4 December. Beaverbrook and Crewe state he met Chamberlain, Curzon and Cecil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook441_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaverbrook441-378">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b133_379-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b133-379">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> Cassar follows these opinions, to a degree.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar223_380-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar223-380">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> But Chamberlain himself was adamant that he and his colleagues met Asquith only once during the crisis and that was on the following day, Tuesday 5 December. Chamberlain wrote at the time, "On Tuesday afternoon the Prime Minister sent for Curzon, Bob Cecil and myself. This is the first and only time the three of us met Asquith during those fateful days."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChamberlain123_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChamberlain123-381">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> His recollection is supported by details of their meetings with Bonar Law and other colleagues,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChamberlain123_381-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChamberlain123-381">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> in the afternoon, and then in the evening of the 4th,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindsay372–373_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindsay372–373-382">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> and by most modern historians, e.g. Gilmour<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilmour457_383-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilmour457-383">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup> and Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams234_384-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams234-384">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> Crawford records how little he and his senior Unionist colleagues were involved in the key discussions, and by implication, how much better informed were the press lords, writing in his diary: "We were all in such doubt as to what had actually occurred, and we sent out for an evening paper to see if there was any news!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindsay373_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindsay373-385">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> Asquith certainly did meet his senior Liberal colleagues on the evening of 4 December; they were unanimously opposed to compromise with Lloyd George and supported Asquith's growing determination to fight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar224_377-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar224-377">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup> His way forward had been cleared by his tendering the resignation of his government to the King earlier in the day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar223_380-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar223-380">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> Asquith also saw Bonar Law, who confirmed that he would resign if Asquith failed to implement the War Council agreement as discussed only the day before.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985461_386-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985461-386">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup> In the evening, and having declined two requests for meetings, Asquith threw down the gauntlet to Lloyd George by rejecting the War Council proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985462_387-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985462-387">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tuesday_5_December">Tuesday 5 December</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Tuesday 5 December">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Balfour,_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Arthur_Balfour%2C_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg/170px-Arthur_Balfour%2C_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Arthur_Balfour%2C_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg/255px-Arthur_Balfour%2C_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Arthur_Balfour%2C_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg/340px-Arthur_Balfour%2C_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3811" data-file-height="4520" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Balfour,_photo_portrait_facing_left.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a></div></div></div>
<p>Lloyd George accepted the challenge by return of post, writing: "As all delay is fatal in war, I place my office without further parley at your disposal."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985462_387-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985462-387">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup> Asquith had anticipated this response, but was surprised by a letter from Arthur Balfour, who until that point had been removed from the crisis by illness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins453_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins453-388">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup> On its face, this letter merely offered confirmation that Balfour believed that Lloyd George's scheme for a smaller War Council deserved a chance and that he had no wish to remain at the Admiralty if Lloyd George wished him out. Jenkins argues that Asquith should have recognised it as a shift of allegiance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins453_388-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins453-388">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup> Asquith discussed the crisis with Lord Crewe and they agreed an early meeting with the Unionist ministers was essential. Without their support, "it would be impossible for Asquith to continue."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar226_389-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar226-389">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's meeting with Chamberlain, Curzon and Cecil at 3.00 p.m. only highlighted the weakness of his position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDutton133_364-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDutton133-364">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> They unanimously declined to serve in a Government that did not include Bonar Law and Lloyd George,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins454_390-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins454-390">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup> as a Government so constituted offered no "prospect of stability". Their reply to Asquith's follow-up question as to whether they would serve under Lloyd George caused him even more concern. The "Three Cs" stated they would serve under Lloyd George if he could create the stable Government they considered essential for the effective prosecution of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChamberlain124_391-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChamberlain124-391">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup> The end was near, and a further letter from Balfour declining to reconsider his earlier decision brought it about. The Home Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Samuel,_1st_Viscount_Samuel" title="Herbert Samuel, 1st Viscount Samuel">Herbert Samuel</a>, recorded in a contemporaneous note: "We were all strongly of opinion, from which [Asquith] did not dissent, that there was no alternative [to resignation]. We could not carry on without LlG and the Unionists and ought not to give the appearance of wishing to do so."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel122_392-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel122-392">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> At 7.00&#160;pm, having been Prime Minister for eight years and 241 days, Asquith went to Buckingham Palace and tendered his resignation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b134_393-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b134-393">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup> Describing the event to a friend sometime later, Asquith wrote; "When I fully realised what a position had been created, I saw that I could not go on without dishonour or impotence, or both."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1933241_394-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1933241-394">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup> That evening, he dined at Downing Street with family and friends, his daughter-in-law Cynthia describing the scene: "I sat next to the P.M.—he was too darling—rubicund, serene, puffing a guinea cigar and talking of going to Honolulu."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECynthia_Asquith241_395-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECynthia_Asquith241-395">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup> Cynthia believed that he would be back "in the saddle" within a fortnight with his position strengthened.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Courcy2014330–340_396-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Courcy2014330–340-396">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later that evening Bonar Law, who had been to the Palace to receive the King's commission, arrived to enquire whether Asquith would serve under him. Lord Crewe described Asquith's reply as "altogether discouraging, if not definitely in the negative."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b134_393-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b134-393">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-398" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-398">&#91;o&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wednesday_6_December">Wednesday 6 December</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Wednesday 6 December">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%;&#32;; background-color: #c6dbf7;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style="">I am personally very sorry for poor old Squiff.  He has had a hard time and even when 'exhilarated' seems to have had more capacity and brain power than any of the others.  However, I expect more action and less talk is needed now</div>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">General Douglas Haig on Asquith's fall (6 December)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield_&amp;_Bourne259_399-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield_&amp;_Bourne259-399">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Wednesday saw an afternoon conference at Buckingham Palace, hosted by the King and chaired by Balfour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins455_400-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins455-400">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> There is some doubt as to the originator of the idea,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins455_400-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins455-400">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> although Adams considers that it was Bonar Law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams238_401-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams238-401">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup> This is supported by a handwritten note of Aitken's, reproduced in <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A.J.P. Taylor's</a> life of that politician, which reads: "6th Wed. Meeting at BL house with G. (Lloyd George) and C. (Carson)—Decide on Palace Conference."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor107_402-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor107-402">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, Crewe suggests that the suggestion came jointly from Lord Derby and Edwin Montagu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPope-Hennessy187_403-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPope-Hennessy187-403">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> However it came about, it did not bring the compromise the King sought. Within two hours of its break-up, Asquith, after consulting his Liberal colleagues,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss222_404-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss222-404">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup> except for Lloyd George, declined to serve under Bonar Law,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams238_401-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams238-401">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup> who accordingly declined the King's commission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar231_405-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar231-405">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup> At 7.00&#160;pm. Lloyd George was invited to form a Government. In just over twenty four hours he had done so, forming a small <a href="/wiki/War_Cabinet#First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="War Cabinet">War Cabinet</a> instead of the mooted War Council, and at 7.30 p.m. on Thursday 7 December he kissed hands as Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985481_406-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985481-406">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup> His achievement in creating a government was considerable, given that almost all of the senior Liberals sided with Asquith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung370_407-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung370-407">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup> Balfour's acceptance of the Foreign Office made it possible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEgremont280_408-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEgremont280-408">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup> Others placed a greater responsibility on Asquith as the author of his own downfall, Churchill writing; "A fierce, resolute Asquith, fighting with all his powers would have conquered easily. But the whole trouble arose from the fact that there was no fierce resolute Asquith to win this war or any other."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor119_409-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor119-409">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Wartime_Opposition_Leader:_1916.E2.80.931918"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime_Opposition_Leader:_1916–1918">Wartime Opposition Leader: 1916–1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Wartime Opposition Leader: 1916–1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Asquiths finally vacated 10 Downing Street on 9 December. Asquith, not normally given to displays of emotion, confided to his wife that he felt he had been stabbed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Courcy2014330–40_410-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Courcy2014330–40-410">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup> He likened himself (10 December) to the Biblical character <a href="/wiki/Job_(biblical_figure)" title="Job (biblical figure)">Job</a>, although he also commented that <a href="/wiki/Aristide_Briand" title="Aristide Briand">Aristide Briand</a>'s government was also under strain in France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins461–2_411-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins461–2-411">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup> Lord Newton wrote in his diary of meeting Asquith at dinner a few days after the fall; "It became painfully evident that he was suffering from an incipient nervous breakdown and before leaving the poor man completely collapsed."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewton230_412-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewton230-412">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was particularly appalled at Balfour's behaviour,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung371_413-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung371-413">&#91;398&#93;</a></sup> especially as he had argued against Lloyd George to retain Balfour at the Admiralty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEgremont281_414-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEgremont281-414">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup> Writing years later, Margot's spleen was still evident; "between you and me, this is what hurt my husband more than anything else. That Lloyd George (a Welshman!) should betray him, he dimly did understand, but that Arthur should join his enemy and help to ruin him, he never understood."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEgremont281_414-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEgremont281-414">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's fall was met with rejoicing in much of the British and Allied press and sterling rallied against the German mark on the New York markets. Press attacks on Asquith continued and indeed increased after the publication of the Dardanelles Report.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss224-7_415-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss224-7-415">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a> after 1846, Asquith after 1916 still controlled the party machinery and resented those who had ousted him, but showed no real interest in reuniting his party. Asquith did not put any pressure on Liberals to eschew joining the coalition government; in fact, though, few Liberals did join it. Most Liberal parliamentarians remained intensely loyal to him, and felt that he alone should not be left to face the criticism. On 8 December a gathering of Liberal MPs gave Asquith a vote of confidence as Leader of the Liberal Party, followed unanimously a few days later by the executive of the National Liberal Federation. There was much hostility to Lloyd George at these gatherings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss225-8_416-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss225-8-416">&#91;401&#93;</a></sup>
