</p><p>Within Parliament, Asquith pursued a course of quiet support, retaining a "heavy, continuing responsibility for the decision of August 4, 1914."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins465_417-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins465-417">&#91;402&#93;</a></sup> Gardiner in the <i>Daily News</i> (9 December) stated explicitly that Lloyd George's government should not have to live under the constant barrage of criticism that Asquith's coalition had endured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss227-8_418-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss227-8-418">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup> In a "gracious" reply to Lloyd George's first speech in the House of Commons as Prime Minister on 19 December 1916, Asquith made clear that he did not see his role "in any sense to be the leader of what is called an opposition".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_200220_419-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_200220-419">&#91;404&#93;</a></sup> From around the spring of 1917 Asquith's reluctance to criticise the government at all began to exasperate some of his press supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss227-8_418-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss227-8-418">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of the Commons, Margot and he returned to 20 Cavendish Square and he divided his life between there, The Wharf and visiting. Money, in the absence of his premier's salary, became more of a concern.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins466_420-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins466-420">&#91;405&#93;</a></sup> In March 1917 he was informally offered the Lord Chancellorship, with the highest salary in government, but he declined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins467_421-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins467-421">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup> Personal sadness continued in December 1917 when Asquith's third son <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Asquith" title="Arthur Asquith">Arthur</a>, known in the family as "Oc", was badly wounded fighting in France; his leg was amputated in January 1918. Asquith's daughter-in-law recorded in her diary; "The Old Boy (Asquith) sent me fifteen pounds and also, in a letter, told me the sad news of poor, dear Oc having been badly wounded again."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECynthia_Asquith384_422-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECynthia_Asquith384-422">&#91;407&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maurice_Debate">Maurice Debate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Maurice Debate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Debate" title="Maurice Debate">Maurice Debate</a></div>
<p>On 7 May 1918 a letter from a serving officer, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Barton_Maurice" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Barton Maurice">Major-General Sir Frederick Maurice</a> appeared in four London newspapers, accusing Lloyd George and Bonar Law of having misled the House of Commons in debates the previous month as to the manpower strength of the army in France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins467_421-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins467-421">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup> Asquith, who received a letter from Maurice on 6 May,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford451_423-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford451-423">&#91;408&#93;</a></sup> and had also been in contact with the sacked Robertson,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodward209–210_424-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodward209–210-424">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup> with whom Maurice discussed the letter, called for a Select Committee of the House to investigate the charges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford453_425-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford453-425">&#91;410&#93;</a></sup> In response to a private notice question, Bonar Law had offered a judicial inquiry, with Asquith free to choose the judges, but Asquith declined this offer on the evening of 7 May, thinking it contrary to the dignity of Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002493_426-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002493-426">&#91;411&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the debate, he received a surprising communication (8 May) from <a href="/wiki/H._A._Gwynne" title="H. A. Gwynne">H. A. Gwynne</a>, the editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Morning_Post" title="The Morning Post">The Morning Post</a></i>, and previously a fervent opponent. "The effect of the Maurice letter, and your motion, must be the dissolution of the present government (and) your accession to power."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002494_427-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002494-427">&#91;412&#93;</a></sup> At this point "Asquith hated Lloyd George with a passion" but he did not want the premiership for himself.<sup id="cite_ref-428" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-428">&#91;413&#93;</a></sup>  Asquith's opening speech on the Select Committee motion was lengthy and lacked punch. Bridgeman recorded; "He did not make much of a case, and did not even condemn Maurice's breach of the King's Regulations, for which he got a very heavy blow from L.G.".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBridgeman132_429-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBridgeman132-429">&#91;414&#93;</a></sup> Lloyd George's one and a quarter-hour long reply was "a stunning solo display by the greatest rhetorician of his age"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams272_430-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams272-430">&#91;415&#93;</a></sup> in which he threatened the House with the inevitable political consequence of a vote for Asquith's motion. "… if this motion is carried, he [Asquith] will again be responsible for the conduct of the War. Make no mistake!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002498_431-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002498-431">&#91;416&#93;</a></sup> John Ramsden summed up the opinion in the House of Commons; "Lloyd George's lies were (preferred to) Asquith's half-measures."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsden115_432-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsden115-432">&#91;417&#93;</a></sup> The motion was defeated by 293 votes to 106, more an "utter rejection of Asquith, than (a) wholehearted endorsement of Lloyd George",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002499_433-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_2002499-433">&#91;418&#93;</a></sup> and the latter's position in Parliament was not seriously threatened for the remainder of the War.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_war">End of the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: End of the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_11_November_1918" title="Armistice of 11 November 1918">Armistice of 11 November 1918</a></div>
<p>Asquith was left politically discredited by the Maurice Debate and by the clear turn of the war in the Allies' favour from the summer of 1918. He devoted far more effort to his <a href="/wiki/Romanes_Lecture#1910s" title="Romanes Lecture">Romanes Lecture</a> "Some Aspects of the Victorian Age" at Oxford in June 1918 than to any political speech. However, Lady Ottoline Morrell thought it "a dull address".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss228-30_434-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss228-30-434">&#91;419&#93;</a></sup> A letter of July 1918 describes a typical couple of days. "Nothing much is happening here. I dined with the usual crowd at Mrs. Astor's last night. The Duke of Connaught lunches here on Friday: don't you wish you were coming!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_193367–68_435-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_193367–68-435">&#91;420&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The beginning of the end of the war began where it had begun, with the last German offensive on the Western Front, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="Second Battle of the Marne">Second Battle of the Marne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart531_436-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart531-436">&#91;421&#93;</a></sup> "The tide of German success was stemmed and the ebb began under pressure of the great Allied counter-stroke."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart531_436-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart531-436">&#91;421&#93;</a></sup> In response to the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied</a> offensives, "the governments of the Central Powers were everywhere in collapse".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams273_437-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams273-437">&#91;422&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Decline_and_eclipse:_1918.E2.80.931926"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_eclipse:_1918–1926">Decline and eclipse: 1918–1926</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Decline and eclipse: 1918–1926">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coupon_election">Coupon election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Coupon election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even before the Armistice, Lloyd George had been considering the political landscape and, on 2 November 1918, wrote to Bonar Law proposing an immediate election with a formal endorsement—for which Asquith coined the name "Coupon", with overtones of wartime food rationing—for Coalition candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins475_438-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins475-438">&#91;423&#93;</a></sup> News of his plans soon reached Asquith, causing considerable concern. On 6 November he wrote to Hilda Henderson; "I suppose that tomorrow we shall be told the final decision about this accursed election."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_193381_439-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_193381-439">&#91;424&#93;</a></sup> A Liberal delegation met Lloyd George in the week of 6 November to propose Liberal reunification but was swiftly rebuffed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins475–6_440-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins475–6-440">&#91;425&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_193381_439-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_193381-439">&#91;424&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith joined in the celebrations of the Armistice, speaking in the Commons, attending the service of thanksgiving at <a href="/wiki/St_Margaret%27s,_Westminster" title="St Margaret&#39;s, Westminster">St Margaret's, Westminster</a> and afterwards lunching with King George.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins476_441-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins476-441">&#91;426&#93;</a></sup> Asquith had a friendly meeting with Lloyd George a few days after the Armistice (the exact date is unclear), which Lloyd George began by saying "I understand you don't wish to join the government." <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss236_442-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss236-442">&#91;427&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was instead keen to go to <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">the Peace Conference</a>, where he considered his expertise at finance and international law would have been an asset.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsden139_443-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsden139-443">&#91;428&#93;</a></sup> As he refused to accept public subordination, Lloyd George, despite lobbying from the King and Churchill, refused to invite him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins477_444-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins477-444">&#91;429&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss236_442-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss236-442">&#91;427&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith led the Liberal Party into the election, but with a singular lack of enthusiasm, writing on 25 November: "I doubt whether there is much interest. The whole thing is a wicked fraud."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins477_444-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins477-444">&#91;429&#93;</a></sup> The Liberal leaders expected to lose the <a href="/wiki/1918_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1918 United Kingdom general election">1918 election</a> badly, as they had lost the "Khaki Election" in 1900, but did not foresee the sheer scale of the defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9_445-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9-445">&#91;430&#93;</a></sup> Asquith hoped for 100 Liberal MPs to be returned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins478_446-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins478-446">&#91;431&#93;</a></sup> He began by attacking the Conservatives, but was eventually driven to attack the "blank cheque" which the government was demanding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9_445-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9-445">&#91;430&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith was one of five people given a free pass by the Coalition but the East Fife Unionist Association defied national instructions and put up a candidate, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sprot" title="Alexander Sprot">Alexander Sprot</a>, against him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9_445-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9-445">&#91;430&#93;</a></sup> Sprot was refused a Coalition "coupon".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins479_447-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins479-447">&#91;432&#93;</a></sup> Asquith assumed his own seat would be safe and spent only two and half days there, speaking only to closed meetings; in one speech there on 11 December he conceded that he did not want to "displace" the current government. He scoffed at press rumours that he was being barracked by a gang of discharged soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9_445-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss236–9-445">&#91;430&#93;</a></sup> Postwar reconstruction, the desire for harsh peace terms, and Asquith's desire to attend the peace talks, were campaign issues, with posters asking: "Asquith nearly lost you the War. Are you going to let him spoil the Peace?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss240_448-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss240-448">&#91;433&#93;</a></sup> James Scott, his chairman at East Fife, wrote of "a swarm of women going from door to door indulging in a slander for which they had not a shadow of proof. This was used for such a purpose as to influence the female vote very much against you."<sup id="cite_ref-449" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-449">&#91;p&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins481_450-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins481-450">&#91;434&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the poll on 14 December, Lloyd George's coalition won a landslide, with Asquith and every other former Liberal Cabinet minister losing his seat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter99_451-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter99-451">&#91;435&#93;</a></sup> Margot later recorded having telephoned Liberal headquarters for the results; "Give me the East Fife figures: Asquith 6994—Sprott [<i>sic</i>] 8996." She said she had exclaimed "Asquith beat? … Thank God!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962334_452-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962334-452">&#91;436&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_Birrell" title="Augustine Birrell">Augustine Birrell</a> also wrote to him "You are surely better off out of it for the time, than watching Ll.G. lead apes to Hell".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins480_453-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins480-453">&#91;437&#93;</a></sup> But for Asquith personally, "the blow was crippling, a personal humiliation which destroyed his hope of exercising any influence on the peace settlement."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins479_447-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins479-447">&#91;432&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1919:_out_of_Parliament">1919: out of Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: 1919: out of Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg/200px-Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg/300px-Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg/400px-Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="571" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cluysenaar_Asquith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1919 portrait by <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Cluysenaar" title="André Cluysenaar">André Cluysenaar</a></div></div></div>
<p>Asquith remained leader of the Liberal Party, despite McKenna vainly urging him, almost immediately after the election, to offer his resignation to the National Liberal Federation and help with building an alliance with Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss240_448-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss240-448">&#91;433&#93;</a></sup> At first Asquith was extremely unpopular, and there is no evidence that he was invited to address any Liberal Association anywhere in the country for the first six months of 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins483_454-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins483-454">&#91;438&#93;</a></sup> He continued to be calumnied in the press and Parliament over the supposed presence of Germans in Downing Street during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss243_455-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss243-455">&#91;439&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although accounts differ as to the exact numbers, around 29 uncouponed Liberals had been elected, only three with any junior ministerial experience, not all of them opponents of the coalition. There was widespread discontent at Asquith's leadership, and Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bramsdon" title="Thomas Bramsdon">T. A. Bramsdon</a>, who said that he had been elected at Portsmouth only by promising <i>not</i> to support Asquith, protested openly at his remaining leader from outside the Commons. At first Lloyd George extended the government whip to <i>all</i> Liberal MPs. On 3 February 23 non-coalition Liberals formed themselves into a "Free Liberal" group (soon known as the "Wee Frees" after a <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(since_1900)" title="Free Church of Scotland (since 1900)">Scottish religious sect of that name</a>); they accepted Asquith's appointment of <a href="/wiki/Donald_Maclean_(British_politician)" title="Donald Maclean (British politician)">Sir Donald Maclean</a> as chairman in his absence but insisted that <a href="/wiki/George_Rennie_Thorne" class="mw-redirect" title="George Rennie Thorne">George Rennie Thorne</a>, whom Asquith had appointed Chief Whip, hold that job jointly with <a href="/wiki/James_Myles_Hogge" class="mw-redirect" title="James Myles Hogge">James Myles Hogge</a>, of whom Asquith and Maclean had a low opinion. After a brief attempt to set up a joint committee with the Coalition Liberal MPs to explore reunion, the "Wee Frees" resigned the government whip on 4 April, although some Liberal MPs still remained of uncertain allegiance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss241–242_456-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss241–242-456">&#91;440&#93;</a></sup> The Liberals won by-elections in March and April 1919, but thereafter Labour performed better than the Liberals in by-elections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss242–244_457-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss242–244-457">&#91;441&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1919 Asquith gave a weak speech to Liberal candidates, his first public speech since the election. In Newcastle (15 May) he gave a slightly stronger speech, encouraged by his audience to "Hit Out!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss244_458-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss244-458">&#91;442&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was also disappointed by the "terms and spirit" of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> in May, but did not oppose it very strongly in public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss243_455-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss243-455">&#91;439&#93;</a></sup> On 31 July 1919, after a lunch in honour of former Supreme Allied Commander <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Foch" title="Ferdinand Foch">Ferdinand Foch</a>, Asquith wrote "he talked a lot of nonsense about Germany sinking never to rise again."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins481_450-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins481-450">&#91;434&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1919 Asquith was asked to preside over a Royal Commission into the Universities of Oxford and Cambridge, although the report when it came was, in line with Asquith's own academic views, somewhat conservative.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins483_454-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins483-454">&#91;438&#93;</a></sup> The commission began hearings in January 1920; many dons would have preferred Haldane as chair.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss244_458-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss244-458">&#91;442&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's public rehabilitation continued with the receipt in late 1919 of the <a href="/wiki/1914_Star" title="1914 Star">1914 Star</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_War_Medal" title="British War Medal">British War Medal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Victory_Medal_(United_Kingdom)" title="Victory Medal (United Kingdom)">Victory Medal</a>, honours which the War Office, under Churchill, had originally intended only to be awarded to Lloyd George, until the King insisted Asquith receive them also.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins483_454-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins483-454">&#91;438&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Maclean and others urged Asquith to stand in the <a href="/wiki/1919_Spen_Valley_by-election" title="1919 Spen Valley by-election">Spen Valley by-election</a> in December 1919, but it is unclear whether he ever considered the idea. This was just as well, as it had become clear that Labour were going to fight the seat hard and they defeated Sir John Simon when Lloyd George insisted on splitting the Liberal vote by running a Coalition Liberal candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss243_455-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss243-455">&#91;439&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paisley">Paisley</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Paisley">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paisley_by-election,_1920" class="mw-redirect" title="Paisley by-election, 1920">Paisley by-election, 1920</a></div>
<p>A Parliamentary seat was essential if Asquith was again to play any serious part in future events. 
By the autumn of 1919 J.M. Hogge was openly critical of Asquith's leadership, and by January 1920 it was rumoured that he had given Asquith an ultimatum that unless he returned to Parliament in a by-election the Independent Liberal MPs would repudiate him as their leader (had he lost a by-election, his position would have been untenable anyway, as he well knew).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss245_459-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss245-459">&#91;443&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1920, an opportunity arose at <a href="/wiki/Paisley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Paisley (UK Parliament constituency)">Paisley</a>, in Scotland like his previous seat, after the death of the Liberal MP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1933125_460-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1933125-460">&#91;444&#93;</a></sup> The Liberals had held the seat by only 106 votes in 1918. Asquith's adoption was not a foregone conclusion: the local Association was split between pro- and anti-coalition factions, and he was selected by a vote of 20:17 by the executive and then 92:75 of the wider members. He was formally adopted on 21 January 1920 and soon united the local Liberal Association behind him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss242–244_457-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss242–244-457">&#91;441&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins485_461-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins485-461">&#91;445&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was lukewarm at the thought of returning to Scotland, and regarded his gamble with trepidation, although he grew more confident as the campaign progressed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss246–247_462-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss246–247-462">&#91;446&#93;</a></sup> Travelling with Margot, his daughter Violet and a small staff, Asquith directed most of his campaign not against Labour, who were already in second place, but against the Coalition, calling for a less harsh line on German reparations and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins486_463-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins486-463">&#91;447&#93;</a></sup> Some "thought fit to compare [the campaign] with <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">Gladstone's</a> <a href="/wiki/Midlothian_campaign" title="Midlothian campaign">Midlothian campaign</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1933130_464-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1933130-464">&#91;448&#93;</a></sup> although Asquith himself was more circumspect. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss248–249_465-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss248–249-465">&#91;449&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The result was stupendous, with Asquith defeating his Labour opponent by a majority of over 2000 votes, with the Coalition candidate a very poor third.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins487_466-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins487-466">&#91;450&#93;</a></sup> Violet was ecstatic; "every star in the political skies favoured Father when we left Paisley, he became there what he has never before been in his life, the 'popular' candidate, the darling of the crowd."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter113_467-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter113-467">&#91;451&#93;</a></sup> The poll was up by 8,000 from 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins487_466-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins487-466">&#91;450&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's surprise victory was helped by the support of the press baron <a href="/wiki/Harold_Harmsworth,_1st_Viscount_Rothermere" title="Harold Harmsworth, 1st Viscount Rothermere">Lord Rothermere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss247–248_468-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss247–248-468">&#91;452&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He was seen off by tumultuous crowds at Glasgow, and greeted by further crowds at <a href="/wiki/Euston_railway_station" title="Euston railway station">Euston</a> the next morning, and along the road on his first return to Parliament. However, he received only a chilly greeting inside the Chamber, and no personal congratulations from Coalition politicians, except from <a href="/wiki/George_Cave,_1st_Viscount_Cave" title="George Cave, 1st Viscount Cave">Lord Cave</a>, who was later to defeat him for the Chancellorship of Oxford University in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins487–488_469-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins487–488-469">&#91;453&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Leader_of_the_Opposition:_1920.E2.80.931921"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leader_of_the_Opposition:_1920–1921">Leader of the Opposition: 1920–1921</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Leader of the Opposition: 1920–1921">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paisley was a false dawn, for the Liberals and for Asquith personally. Jenkins wrote that "The post-war Liberal day never achieved more than a grey and short-lived light. By 1924, it was dusk again. By 1926, for Asquith, it was political night."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins489_470-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins489-470">&#91;454&#93;</a></sup> Maurice Cowling characterised Asquith at this time as; "a dignified wreck, neither effective in the House of Commons nor attractive as a public reputation, (who) drank too much and (who) had lost touch with the movement of events and the spirit of the time."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowling100_471-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowling100-471">&#91;455&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Money, or its lack, also became an increasing concern. Margot's extravagance was legendary<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford460_472-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford460-472">&#91;456&#93;</a></sup> and Asquith was no longer earning either the legal fees or the prime ministerial salary they had enjoyed in earlier years. Additionally, there were on-going difficulties with Margot's inheritance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford460_472-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford460-472">&#91;456&#93;</a></sup> In 1920, as an economy measure, 20 Cavendish Square was sold<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith371_473-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith371-473">&#91;457&#93;</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Weetman_Pearson,_1st_Viscount_Cowdray" title="Weetman Pearson, 1st Viscount Cowdray">Viscountess Cowdray</a><sup id="cite_ref-474" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-474">&#91;458&#93;</a></sup> and Asquith and Margot moved to 44, <a href="/wiki/Bedford_Square" title="Bedford Square">Bedford Square</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith371_473-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith371-473">&#91;457&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Criticism of Asquith's weak leadership continued. Lloyd George's mistress Frances Stevenson wrote (18 March) that he was "finished … no fight left in him"; the press baron Lord Rothermere, who had supported him at Paisley, wrote on 1 April of his "obvious incapacity for the position he is expected to fill".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss249_475-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss249-475">&#91;459&#93;</a></sup> In fact Asquith spoke in the House of Commons far more frequently than he had ever previously done when not a minister. He also spoke frequently around the country, in June 1921 topping the Liberal Chief Whip's list of the most active speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins498_476-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins498-476">&#91;460&#93;</a></sup> The issue was the quality of his contributions. Asquith still maintained friendly relations with Lloyd George, although Margot made no secret of her enmity for him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss250_477-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss250-477">&#91;461&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the Paisley by-election Asquith had accepted that the next government must be some kind of Liberal-Labour coalition, but Labour had distanced themselves because of his policies on the mines, the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Soviet_War" title="Polish–Soviet War">Russo-Polish War</a>, education, the prewar secret treaties and the suppression of the Easter Rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss251_478-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss251-478">&#91;462&#93;</a></sup> The success of <a href="/wiki/Anti-Waste_League" title="Anti-Waste League">Anti-Waste League</a> candidates at by-elections made leading Liberals feel that there was a strong anti-Coalition vote which might be tapped by a wider-based and more credible opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins490–1_479-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins490–1-479">&#91;463&#93;</a></sup> By late June 1921 Asquith's leadership was still under strong attack from within the Wee Free group, although Frances Stevenson's claim in her diary that most of them now wanted Lloyd George as their leader is not corroborated by the report in <i>The Times</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss252_480-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss252-480">&#91;464&#93;</a></sup> Lord Robert Cecil, a moderate and pro-<a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> Conservative, had been having talks with Edward Grey about a possible coalition, and Asquith and leading Liberals Crewe, Runciman and Maclean had a meeting with them on 5 July 1921, and two subsequent ones. Cecil wanted a genuine coalition rather than a <i>de facto</i> Liberal government, with Grey rather than Asquith as Prime Minister, but the Liberals did not, and little came of the plans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins491–492_481-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins491–492-481">&#91;465&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss251_478-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss251-478">&#91;462&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith did fiercely oppose "the hellish policy of reprisals" in Ireland, impressing the young <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Mosley" title="Oswald Mosley">Oswald Mosley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss252_480-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss252-480">&#91;464&#93;</a></sup> J.M. Hogge even urged Sir Donald Maclean (31 August) to "knock Asquith into the middle of next week" and seize back the chairmanship of the Liberal MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss250_477-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss250-477">&#91;461&#93;</a></sup> Late in 1921 the National Liberal Federation adopted an industrial programme without Asquith's agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss255_482-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss255-482">&#91;466&#93;</a></sup> On 24 October 1921 Asquith commented "if one tries to strike a bold true note half one's friends shiver and cower, and implore one not to get in front of the band".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss250_477-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss250-477">&#91;461&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leader_of_the_Opposition:_1922">Leader of the Opposition: 1922</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Leader of the Opposition: 1922">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In January 1922 <a href="/wiki/C.P._Scott" class="mw-redirect" title="C.P. Scott">C.P. Scott</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Guardian" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester Guardian">Manchester Guardian</a></i> told Asquith that he supported a centre-left grouping, but only if moderate Labour was included—in reality Labour leaders were unable to deliver the support of their local members for such a realignment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins492–493_483-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins492–493-483">&#91;467&#93;</a></sup> Asquith achieved more success with a major speech at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Central_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Central Hall">Westminster Central Hall</a> in January 1922, in reply to a speech by Lloyd George a few days earlier. Asquith had with some difficulty been persuaded to make the maximum possible reference to his renewed alliance with Grey, but Haldane had refused to join the platform. Five days later Churchill replied with a pro-Coalition speech in which he accused Asquith and other Liberals of having "stood carefully aside" during the war, causing deep offence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss253–255_484-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss253–255-484">&#91;468&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-486" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-486">&#91;q&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the summer of 1922 Asquith's interest in politics was at a very low ebb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins493_487-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins493-487">&#91;470&#93;</a></sup> He was observed to be "<a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication" title="Alcohol intoxication">very heavily loaded</a>" and was helped up the stairs by Lloyd George at a party of Sir <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sassoon" title="Philip Sassoon">Philip Sassoon</a>'s on 16 July 1922, whilst his reputation was further damaged by his portrayal in <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Crome_Yellow" title="Crome Yellow">Crome Yellow</a></i> and by the publication of the first volume of Margot's memoirs, which sold well in the UK and the USA, but were thought an undignified way for a former Prime Minister to make money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss255–256_488-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss255–256-488">&#91;471&#93;</a></sup> On 13 September 1922 Sir Donald Maclean told <a href="/wiki/Harold_Laski" title="Harold Laski">Harold Laski</a> that Asquith was devoted to bridge and small talk and did not do enough real work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss255_482-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss255-482">&#91;466&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was increasingly attracted by the thought of making money from writing, with Churchill doing very well from his <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Crisis" title="The World Crisis">The World Crisis</a></i> and Lloyd George rumoured to be being paid handsomely for his memoirs (which in the event did not appear until the mid-1930s).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins494_489-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins494-489">&#91;472&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's books <i>The Genesis of the War</i> finally appeared in September 1923 and <i>Studies and Sketches</i> in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins495_490-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins495-490">&#91;473&#93;</a></sup> His second son Herbert recorded; "A large part of my father's later years was occupied with authorship and it was during this period that he wrote most of his longer books."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith367_491-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith367-491">&#91;474&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith played no part in Lloyd George's fall from power in October 1922, which happened because the rank-and-file majority of his Tory coalition partners, led by <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a> and Lloyd George's former colleague Bonar Law, deserted him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams327–328_492-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams327–328-492">&#91;475&#93;</a></sup> Bonar Law formed a purely Conservative government, and the following month, at the <a href="/wiki/1922_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1922 United Kingdom general election">1922 general election</a>, Asquith ceased to be Leader of the Opposition as more Labour MPs were elected than the two Liberal factions combined. 138 Labour members outnumbered the combined Liberal number of 117, with 60 Asquith supporters and 57 "<a href="/wiki/National_Liberal_Party_(UK,_1922)" title="National Liberal Party (UK, 1922)">National Liberals</a>" (adherents to Lloyd George).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins496_493-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins496-493">&#91;476&#93;</a></sup> Asquith had thought Paisley would be safe but was only narrowly returned with a 316 majority (50.5 per cent of the votes cast in a two-candidate battle with Labour), despite a rise in the Liberal vote. He put this down to the 5,000 unemployed at Paisley after the <a href="/wiki/Depression_of_1920%E2%80%9321" title="Depression of 1920–21">slump of 1920–1921</a>. He wrote that he "gloated" over the senior Coalition Liberals—Churchill, <a href="/wiki/Hamar_Greenwood,_1st_Viscount_Greenwood" title="Hamar Greenwood, 1st Viscount Greenwood">Hamar Greenwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Guest" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Guest">Freddie Guest</a> and Edwin Montagu—who lost their seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins495–496_494-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins495–496-494">&#91;477&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-results_495-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-results-495">&#91;478&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_reunion">Liberal reunion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Liberal reunion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 1923 a petition for reunion among Liberal backbenchers received 73 signatures, backed by the Lloyd Georgeite <i>Daily Chronicle</i> and the Asquithian <i>Liberal Magazine</i>. But reunion was opposed by senior Asquithian Liberals like Sir John Simon, Viscount Gladstone and Charles Masterman, and as late as 30 June by journalists such as Massingham and Gardiner of <i>The Nation</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss259–261_496-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss259–261-496">&#91;479&#93;</a></sup> Viscount Gladstone felt that "it was generally recognised that Asquith was no longer effective as an active leader" but that Lloyd George must not succeed him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss259–261_496-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss259–261-496">&#91;479&#93;</a></sup> By July Asquith was superficially friendly to Lloyd George and consulted him, but he did not include him in the Shadow Cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-497" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-497">&#91;r&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss259–261_496-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss259–261-496">&#91;479&#93;</a></sup> Asquith wanted Lloyd George to make the first move but although the latter put out feelers to senior Asquith supporters he insisted that he was "neither a suppliant nor a penitent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins498_476-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins498-476">&#91;460&#93;</a></sup> M.S.R. Kinnear writes that Asquith felt that with Lloyd George's faction declining in strength he had everything to gain by waiting, while too quick an approach would antagonise the Labour leaders who hated Lloyd George and whose support he might need for a future Lib-Lab coalition. Kinnear also argues that Asquith's "gloating" over the defeat of Coalition Liberals in 1922 is evidence that "the most important factor influencing Asquith against quick reunion was his personal dislike of Lloyd George and his desire for vengeance."<sup id="cite_ref-498" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-498">&#91;480&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The political situation was transformed when Baldwin, now Prime Minister, came out in favour of <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">Protection</a> at Plymouth on 22 October 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins498_476-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins498-476">&#91;460&#93;</a></sup> Coming out for Free Trade himself, Lloyd George was obliged, at least formally, to submit to Asquith's leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor219_499-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor219-499">&#91;481&#93;</a></sup> Parliament was dissolved. Asquith and Lloyd George reached agreement on 13 November, followed by a Free Trade manifesto, followed by a more general one. Lloyd George, accompanied by his daughter <a href="/wiki/Megan_Lloyd_George" title="Megan Lloyd George">Megan</a>, came to Paisley to speak in Asquith's support on 24 November.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263_500-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263-500">&#91;482&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith fought an energetic national campaign on free trade in 1923, with echoes of 1903.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins499_501-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins499-501">&#91;483&#93;</a></sup>
He spoke at Nottingham and Manchester, but did not privately expect more than 200 Liberals to be elected—although he hoped to overtake Labour and become Leader of the Opposition once again—and hoped for Baldwin to win by a tiny majority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263_500-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263-500">&#91;482&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The poll at Paisley was split by an independent extreme socialist and a Tory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins498_476-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins498-476">&#91;460&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263_500-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263-500">&#91;482&#93;</a></sup> Asquith won with 33.4 per cent of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-results_495-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-results-495">&#91;478&#93;</a></sup> Nationally, the outcome of the <a href="/wiki/1923_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1923 United Kingdom general election">election in December 1923</a> was a hung Parliament (258 Conservatives, 191 Labour, 158 Liberals); the Liberals had gained seats but were still in third place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins498_476-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins498-476">&#91;460&#93;</a></sup> A quarter of the seats were held by majority less than 1,000. In general, Asquith Liberals did better than Lloyd George Liberals, which Gladstone and Maclean saw as a reason to prevent close co-operation between the factions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263_500-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss261–263-500">&#91;482&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Putting_Labour_in_power">Putting Labour in power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Putting Labour in power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There was no question of the Liberals supporting a continuation of the Conservative government, not least as it was feared that an alliance of the two "bourgeois" parties would antagonise Labour. Asquith commented that "If a Labour Government is ever to be tried in this country, as it will be sooner or later, it could hardly be tried under safer conditions". Asquith's decision to support a minority Labour Government was seconded by Lloyd George and approved by a party meeting on 18 December.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins500_502-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins500-502">&#91;484&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Baldwin's view was similar, as he rejected <a href="/wiki/Robert_Horne,_1st_Viscount_Horne_of_Slamannan" title="Robert Horne, 1st Viscount Horne of Slamannan">Sir Robert Horne</a>'s scheme for a Conservative-Liberal pact. Roy Douglas called the decision to put in Ramsay MacDonald "the most disastrous single action ever performed by a Liberal towards his party." Other historians such as Trevor Wilson and Koss reject this view, arguing that Asquith had little choice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss264–265_503-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss264–265-503">&#91;485&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith was never in doubt as to the correctness of his approach, although a deluge of correspondence urged him to save the country from Socialism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins501_504-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins501-504">&#91;486&#93;</a></sup> He wrote on 28 December "I have been intreated during these weeks, cajoled, wheedled, almost caressed, tortured, threatened, brow-beaten and all but blackmailed to step in as the saviour of society."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiddlemas_&amp;_Barnes253_505-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiddlemas_&amp;_Barnes253-505">&#91;487&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins500_502-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins500-502">&#91;484&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Liberals thus supported Britain's first ever (minority) <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> Government under <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>. The Liberal Party voted for the Labour amendment to the Address, causing Baldwin to resign (Asquith believed that Baldwin could have ignored the vote and carried on attempting to govern without a majority). He thought the new Labour Government "a beggarly array" although he remarked that the Foreign Office staff were glad to see the back of "the Archduke Curzon".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins501_504-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins501-504">&#91;486&#93;</a></sup> Asquith believed that MacDonald would soon be discredited both in the eyes of the country and of his own more extreme supporters, and the Liberal revival would continue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss265_506-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss265-506">&#91;488&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_government_and_the_Campbell_Case">Labour government and the Campbell Case</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Labour government and the Campbell Case">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Asquith's decision only hastened his party's destruction, the Conservative Austen Chamberlain writing to his colleague <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hoare,_1st_Viscount_Templewood" title="Samuel Hoare, 1st Viscount Templewood">Sir Samuel Hoare</a>; "We have got (unexpectedly and by our own blunders and Asquith's greater folly) a second chance. Have we got the wit to take it?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsden183_507-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsden183-507">&#91;489&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Relations with Labour soon became very tense, with Liberal MPs increasingly angered at having to support a Labour Government which treated them with such open hostility. Many Liberals were also angered at MacDonald's pursuit of a trade agreement with the USSR, although Asquith rather less so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins502_508-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins502-508">&#91;490&#93;</a></sup> The intervention of a Labour candidate at a <a href="/wiki/1924_Oxford_by-election" title="1924 Oxford by-election">by-election in Oxford in June</a> handed the seat to the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss266_509-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss266-509">&#91;491&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As Asquith brought MacDonald in so, later in the same year, he had significant responsibility for forcing him out over the <a href="/wiki/Campbell_Case" title="Campbell Case">Campbell Case</a> and the Russian Treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarquand373_510-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarquand373-510">&#91;492&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives proposed a vote of censure against the Government for withdrawing their prosecution for sedition against the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Worker_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Worker (UK)">Daily Worker</a></i>, and Asquith moved an amendment calling for a select committee (the same tactic he had employed over the <a href="/wiki/Marconi_scandal" title="Marconi scandal">Marconi scandal</a> and the Maurice Debate).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins502_508-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins502-508">&#91;490&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's contribution to the debate showed an increasingly rare return to Parliamentary form. "Almost every one of his delightful sentences filled the Chamber with laughter."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarquand376_511-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarquand376-511">&#91;493&#93;</a></sup>
Asquith's motion was passed by 364–198.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins502_508-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins502-508">&#91;490&#93;</a></sup> As in the Maurice Debate, his sense of political tactics was, in Jenkins' view, overcome by his sense of Parliamentary propriety. He could not bring himself to withdraw the amendment, but could not support the government either.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins503_512-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins503-512">&#91;494&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1924_election">1924 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: 1924 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Instead of resigning MacDonald requested, and was granted, a General Election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins502_508-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins502-508">&#91;490&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1924_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1924 United Kingdom general election">1924 election</a> was intended by MacDonald to cripple the Liberals, and it did.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss266_509-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss266-509">&#91;491&#93;</a></sup> Lloyd George refused to hand over money from his fund until he had more say over the Liberal whips office, Liberal Party Headquarters at Arlington Street and an election there was a chance of winning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins503_512-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins503-512">&#91;494&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss266_509-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss266-509">&#91;491&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meetings at Paisley were tumultuous and Asquith was barracked by hecklers singing "<a href="/wiki/The_Red_Flag" title="The Red Flag">The Red Flag</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins504_513-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins504-513">&#91;495&#93;</a></sup> Asquith was widely expected to lose his seat and did so by 2,228.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss267–268_514-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss267–268-514">&#91;496&#93;</a></sup> He received 46.5 per cent of the vote in his final parliamentary election, a straight fight against Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-results_495-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-results-495">&#91;478&#93;</a></sup> Violet wrote; "Father was absolutely controlled. He just said to me, 'I'm out by 2,000'."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter164_515-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter164-515">&#91;497&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was a political, as well as a personal, disaster. Baldwin won a landslide victory, with over "400 Conservatives returned and only 40 Liberals",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowling414_516-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowling414-516">&#91;498&#93;</a></sup> far behind Labour which entrenched its position as the "chief party of Opposition."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowling1_517-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowling1-517">&#91;499&#93;</a></sup> Labour's vote actually increased somewhat (partly as a result of their fielding more candidates than before). The Liberal vote collapsed, much of it coalescing to the Conservatives as a result of the scare around the forged <a href="/wiki/Zinoviev_Letter" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinoviev Letter">Zinoviev Letter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins502_508-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins502-508">&#91;490&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Liberal grandees, who hated Lloyd George, did not press Asquith to retire. Sir Robert Hudson and Maclean called on him (31 October) and insisted he firmly keep the chair at the next meeting and nominate the new Chief Whip himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss267–268_514-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss267–268-514">&#91;496&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elevation">Elevation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Elevation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 1924 election was Asquith's last Parliamentary campaign, and there was no realistic chance of a return to the Commons. He told Charles Masterman "I'd sooner go to hell than to Wales," the only part of the country where Liberal support remained strong. The King offered him a peerage (4 November 1924).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins505_518-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins505-518">&#91;500&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss274_519-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss274-519">&#91;501&#93;</a></sup> Asquith felt he was not rich enough to accept, and would have preferred to die a commoner like <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">Pitt</a> or Gladstone. He accepted in January 1925 after a holiday in Egypt with his son Oc. He deliberately chose the title "Earl of Oxford", saying it had a splendid history as the title chosen by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Harley,_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Earl_Mortimer" title="Robert Harley, 1st Earl of Oxford and Earl Mortimer">Robert Harley</a>, a Tory statesman of <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a>'s reign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins506_520-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins506-520">&#91;502&#93;</a></sup> He was thought by some to have delusions of grandeur, Lady Salisbury writing to him that the title was "like a suburban villa calling itself <a href="/wiki/Versailles" class="mw-redirect" title="Versailles">Versailles</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter167_521-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter167-521">&#91;503&#93;</a></sup> Asquith found the controversy amusing but the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Arms" title="College of Arms">College of Heralds</a> insisted that he add <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith" title="Earl of Oxford and Asquith">"and Asquith"</a> to the final title, after protests from Harley's descendants. In practice he was known as "Lord Oxford".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins508_522-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins508-522">&#91;504&#93;</a></sup> He never enjoyed the House of Lords, and thought the quality of debates there poor.
<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins509_523-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins509-523">&#91;505&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1924 the Liberal party had only been able to put up 343 candidates due to lack of money. At one point the Liberal Shadow Cabinet suggested obtaining the opinion of a <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Chancery" title="Court of Chancery">Chancery</a> Lawyer as to whether the Liberal Party was entitled under <a href="/wiki/English_trust_law" title="English trust law">trust law</a> to Lloyd George's money, which he had obtained from the sale of honours.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins512_524-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins512-524">&#91;506&#93;</a></sup> On 29 January 1925, at a two-day London convention, Asquith launched a Million Fund Appeal in an unsuccessful attempt to raise Liberal Party funds independent of Lloyd George.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss271_525-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss271-525">&#91;507&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins513–514_526-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins513–514-526">&#91;508&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford_Chancellor_election,_1925" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Oxford Chancellor election, 1925">University of Oxford Chancellor election, 1925</a></div>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%;&#32;; background-color: #c6dbf7;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style="">I have had a noble offer from <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Breadalbane_and_Holland" title="Earl of Breadalbane and Holland">Lady Bredalbane</a> who proposes to give me her late husband's Garter robes as a present.  I shall jump at this, as it will save me a lot of money</div>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">Asquith on an additional benefit of The Order of the Garter<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1934135_527-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1934135-527">&#91;509&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>One more disappointment remained. In 1925 he stood for the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chancellors_of_the_University_of_Oxford" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Chancellors of the University of Oxford">Chancellorship of Oxford University</a>, vacant on the death of Lord Curzon. He was eminently suited and was described by <a href="/wiki/F._E._Smith,_1st_Earl_of_Birkenhead" title="F. E. Smith, 1st Earl of Birkenhead">Lord Birkenhead</a>, one of his many Tory supporters, as "the greatest living Oxonian."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell709_528-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell709-528">&#91;510&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith suspected he might lose because of country clergy's hostility to Welsh Disestablishment, blaming "<a href="/wiki/Zadok" title="Zadok">Zadok the Priest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abiathar" title="Abiathar">Abiathar the Priest</a>—with their half-literate followers in the rural parsonages". The election was also seen as a settling of party scores and a mockery of his title. He lost to the Tory candidate, <a href="/wiki/George_Cave,_1st_Viscount_Cave" title="George Cave, 1st Viscount Cave">Lord Cave</a>, by 987 votes to 441 on 20 March. He claimed to be "more disappointed than surprised", but his friend <a href="/wiki/Desmond_MacCarthy" title="Desmond MacCarthy">Desmond MacCarthy</a> wrote that it affected him "more than any disappointment, save one, in his life after he ceased to be Prime Minister."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss274–275_529-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss274–275-529">&#91;511&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins511_530-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins511-530">&#91;512&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1925 Asquith accepted the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a> from Baldwin, who was known to be a personal admirer of his.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss274_519-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss274-519">&#91;501&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins510_531-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins510-531">&#91;513&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resignation">Resignation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Resignation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Difficulties continued with Lloyd George, who had been chairman of the Liberal MPs since 1924,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott467_532-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott467-532">&#91;514&#93;</a></sup> over the party leadership and over party funds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith362_533-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith362-533">&#91;515&#93;</a></sup> In the autumn of 1925 Hobhouse, Runciman and the industrialist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Mond,_1st_Baron_Melchett" title="Alfred Mond, 1st Baron Melchett">Sir Alfred Mond</a> protested to Asquith at Lloyd George organising his own campaign for reform of land ownership. Asquith was "not enthusiastic" but Lloyd George ignored him and arranged for Asquith to be sent reports and calculations ("Lord Oxford likes sums" he wrote). At a meeting on 25 November 1925 Grey, Maclean, Simon, Gladstone and Runciman urged Asquith to have a showdown with Lloyd George over money. Asquith wanted to think it over, and at the December 1925 Federation executive he left the meeting before the topic came up. To the horror of his followers Asquith reached an agreement in principle with Lloyd George over land reform on 2 December, then together they presented plans to the National Liberal Federation on 26 February 1926. But, wrote Maclean, "in private Asquith's language about Lloyd George was lurid."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss272–274_534-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss272–274-534">&#91;516&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins513–514_526-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins513–514-526">&#91;508&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1926 Mond withdrew his financial support from the Liberal Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss272–274_534-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss272–274-534">&#91;516&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins513–514_526-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins513–514-526">&#91;508&#93;</a></sup> The loss of wealthy donors and the failure of the Million Fund Appeal further weakened Asquith's position, and there is some evidence that his frequent requests for money irritated donors like Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Perks" title="Robert Perks">Robert Perks</a> who had given a good deal to the Party over the years, and that outside his inner circle of devotees he was bad at keeping on good terms with potential donors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss275_535-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss275-535">&#91;517&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This was followed by a near final breach with Lloyd George over <a href="/wiki/1926_United_Kingdom_general_strike" title="1926 United Kingdom general strike">the General Strike</a>. The Liberal Shadow Cabinet unequivocally backed Baldwin's handling of the strike on 3 May. Asquith viewed the strike as "criminal folly"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss276_536-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss276-536">&#91;518&#93;</a></sup> and condemned it in the House of Lords, whilst in the Commons Sir John Simon declared it to be illegal. But whereas Asquith and Grey both contributed to the <i><a href="/wiki/British_Gazette" title="British Gazette">British Gazette</a></i>, Churchill's pro-government newssheet, Lloyd George, who had not previously expressed a contrary opinion at Shadow Cabinet, wrote an article for the American press more sympathetic to the strikers, and did not attend the Shadow Cabinet on 10 May, sending his apologies on "policy grounds". Asquith at first assumed him to be trying to ingratiate himself with the Churches and Labour, but then (20 May) sent him a public letter rebuking him for not attending the meeting to discuss his opinions with colleagues in private.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins514–516_537-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins514–516-537">&#91;519&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss276–280_538-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss276–280-538">&#91;520&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In private, both sides were incandescent; one of Asquith's colleagues describing him as; "far more indignant at L.G. than I have ever seen",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss277_539-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss277-539">&#91;521&#93;</a></sup> whilst Lloyd George expressed his private feelings in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Frances_Stevenson" title="Frances Stevenson">Frances Stevenson</a> on 24 May "(Asquith) is a silly old man drunk with hidden conceit. When he listens to those poor creatures he has a weakness for gathering around him he generally makes a fool of himself. They are really 'beat'. Dirty dogs—and bitches."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss278_540-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss278-540">&#91;522&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George's letter of 10 May had not been published, making it appear that Asquith had fired the first shot, and Lloyd George sent a moderate public reply, on 25 May. Asquith then wrote another public letter (1 June) stating that he regarded Lloyd George's behaviour as tantamount to resignation, the same as if a Cabinet Minister had refused to abide by the principle of collective responsibility. Twelve leading Liberals (including Grey, Lord Buckmaster, Simon, Maclean and Runciman) wrote in Asquith's support to <i>The Times</i> (1 June). However, Lloyd George had more support amongst the wider party than amongst the grandees. The executive of the National Liberal Federation, despite backing Asquith by 16:8, had already urged a reconciliation in late May, and the London Liberal Candidates' Association (3 June) and the Liberal MPs (8 June) did the same. Asquith had planned to launch a fightback at the National Liberal Federation in Weston-Super-Mare, due on 17 June, but on the eve of the conference he suffered a stroke (12 June) which put him out of action for three months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins514–516_537-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins514–516-537">&#91;519&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss276–280_538-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss276–280-538">&#91;520&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Margot is said to have later claimed that her husband regretted the breach and had acted after several rich donors had threatened to quit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss281_541-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss281-541">&#91;523&#93;</a></sup> Asquith finally resigned the Liberal leadership on 15 October 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins517_542-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins517-542">&#91;524&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Final_years:_1926.E2.80.931928"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years:_1926–1928">Final years: 1926–1928</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Final years: 1926–1928">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith%27s_tomb,_All_Saints_church,_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg" class="image"><img alt="HH Asquith tomb" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Asquith%27s_tomb%2C_All_Saints_church%2C_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg/220px-Asquith%27s_tomb%2C_All_Saints_church%2C_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Asquith%27s_tomb%2C_All_Saints_church%2C_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg/330px-Asquith%27s_tomb%2C_All_Saints_church%2C_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Asquith%27s_tomb%2C_All_Saints_church%2C_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg/440px-Asquith%27s_tomb%2C_All_Saints_church%2C_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith%27s_tomb,_All_Saints_church,_Sutton_Courtenay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362223.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith's grave at Sutton Courtenay</div></div></div>
<p>Asquith filled his retirement with reading, writing, a little golf,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith365_543-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith365-543">&#91;525&#93;</a></sup> travelling and meeting with friends.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins517_542-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins517-542">&#91;524&#93;</a></sup> Since 1918 he had developed an interest in modern painting and sculpture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins517_542-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins517-542">&#91;524&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His health remained reasonable, almost to the end, though financial concerns increasingly beset him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter172_544-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter172-544">&#91;526&#93;</a></sup> A perhaps surprising contributor to an endowment fund established to support Asquith in 1927 was <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Lord Beaverbrook</a> (the former Max Aitken), who contributed £1,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor236_545-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor236-545">&#91;527&#93;</a></sup> Violet was highly embarrassed by her step-mother's attempts to enlist the aid of Aitken, <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Isaacs,_1st_Marquess_of_Reading" title="Rufus Isaacs, 1st Marquess of Reading">Lord Reading</a> and others of her husband's friends and acquaintances. "It is monstrous that other people (should) be made to foot Margot's bridge bills. <b>How</b> she has dragged his name through the mud!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter173_546-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter173-546">&#91;528&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith suffered a second stroke in January 1927,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss282_547-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss282-547">&#91;529&#93;</a></sup> disabling his left leg for a while and leaving him a wheelchair-user for the spring and early summer of 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins518_548-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins518-548">&#91;530&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's last visit was see the widowed Venetia Montagu in Norfolk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith377_549-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith377-549">&#91;531&#93;</a></sup> On his return to The Wharf, in autumn 1927, he was unable to get out of his car and "he was never again able to go upstairs to his own room."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1934Epilogue_550-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1934Epilogue-550">&#91;532&#93;</a></sup> He suffered a third stroke at the end of 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss283_551-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss283-551">&#91;533&#93;</a></sup> His last months were difficult, and he became increasingly confused, his daughter Violet writing; "To watch Father's glorious mind breaking up and sinking—like a great ship—is a pain beyond all my imagining."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter173_546-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter173-546">&#91;528&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Asquith died, aged 75, at The Wharf on the morning of 15 February 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter172_544-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonham_Carter172-544">&#91;526&#93;</a></sup> "He was buried, at his own wish, with great simplicity,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith378_552-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbert_Asquith378-552">&#91;534&#93;</a></sup> in the churchyard of <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Church,_Sutton_Courtenay" title="All Saints&#39; Church, Sutton Courtenay">All Saints'</a> at Sutton Courtenay, his gravestone recording his name, title, and the dates of his birth and death. A blue plaque records his long residence at 20 Cavendish Square<sup id="cite_ref-553" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-553">&#91;535&#93;</a></sup> and a memorial tablet was subsequently erected in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-554" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-554">&#91;536&#93;</a></sup> Viscount Grey, with Haldane Asquith's oldest political friends, wrote; "I have felt (his) death very much: it is true that his work was done but we were very close together for so many years. I saw the beginning of his Parliamentary life; and to witness the close is the end of a long chapter of my own."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrevelyan333_555-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrevelyan333-555">&#91;537&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's will was proven on 9 June 1928, with his estate amounting to  £9345 9s. 2d. (roughly equivalent to £553,054 in 2018)<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_556-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-556">&#91;538&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-probate_557-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-probate-557">&#91;539&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Descendants">Descendants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Descendants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asquith_family" title="Asquith family">Asquith family</a></div>
