<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helena_Bonham_Carter_(Berlin_Film_Festival_2011)_3_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Helena_Bonham_Carter_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2011%29_3_cropped.jpg/170px-Helena_Bonham_Carter_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2011%29_3_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Helena_Bonham_Carter_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2011%29_3_cropped.jpg/255px-Helena_Bonham_Carter_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2011%29_3_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Helena_Bonham_Carter_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2011%29_3_cropped.jpg/340px-Helena_Bonham_Carter_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2011%29_3_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="612" data-file-height="1038" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helena_Bonham_Carter_(Berlin_Film_Festival_2011)_3_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asquith's great-granddaughter, the actress <a href="/wiki/Helena_Bonham_Carter" title="Helena Bonham Carter">Helena Bonham Carter</a></div></div></div>
<p>Asquith had five children by his first wife, Helen, and two surviving children (three others died at birth or in infancy) by his second wife, Margot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith_558-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith-558">&#91;540&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His eldest son Raymond, after an academic career that outstripped his father's<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins30_559-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins30-559">&#91;541&#93;</a></sup> was killed at the Somme in 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins30_559-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins30-559">&#91;541&#93;</a></sup> His second son <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Asquith_(poet)" title="Herbert Asquith (poet)">Herbert</a> (1881–1947) became a writer and poet and married <a href="/wiki/Lady_Cynthia_Asquith" title="Lady Cynthia Asquith">Cynthia Charteris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford173_560-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford173-560">&#91;542&#93;</a></sup> His later life was marred by alcoholism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford474_561-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford474-561">&#91;543&#93;</a></sup> His third son <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Asquith" title="Arthur Asquith">Arthur</a> (1883–1939), became a soldier and businessman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins30_559-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins30-559">&#91;541&#93;</a></sup> His only daughter by his first wife, <a href="/wiki/Violet_Bonham_Carter" title="Violet Bonham Carter">Violet, later Violet Bonham Carter</a> (1887–1969), became a well-regarded writer and a life peeress as Baroness Asquith of Yarnbury.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford475_562-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford475-562">&#91;544&#93;</a></sup> She married Asquith's <a href="/w/index.php?title=Personal_Private_Secretary&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Personal Private Secretary (page does not exist)">Personal Private Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Bonham_Carter" class="mw-redirect" title="Maurice Bonham Carter">Maurice Bonham Carter</a> in 1915. His fourth son <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Asquith,_Baron_Asquith_of_Bishopstone" title="Cyril Asquith, Baron Asquith of Bishopstone">Cyril</a> (1890–1954) was born on the day Asquith became a QC <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith49_563-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith49-563">&#91;545&#93;</a></sup> and later became a <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_Appeal_in_Ordinary" title="Lords of Appeal in Ordinary">Law Lord</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins30_559-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins30-559">&#91;541&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His two children by Margot were <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Bibesco" title="Elizabeth Bibesco">Elizabeth, later Princess Antoine Bibesco</a> (1897–1945), a writer, who also struggled with alcohol<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford476_564-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford476-564">&#91;546&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Asquith" title="Anthony Asquith">Anthony Asquith</a> (1902–1968),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962Appendix_1_565-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_1962Appendix_1-565">&#91;547&#93;</a></sup> known as "Puffin", a film-maker, whose life was also severely affected by alcoholism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClifford476_564-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClifford476-564">&#91;546&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Among his living descendants are his great-granddaughter, the actress <a href="/wiki/Helena_Bonham_Carter" title="Helena Bonham Carter">Helena Bonham Carter</a> (b. 1966),<sup id="cite_ref-566" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-566">&#91;548&#93;</a></sup> and two great-grandsons, <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Asquith" title="Dominic Asquith">Dominic Asquith</a>, British High Commissioner to India since March 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-567" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-567">&#91;549&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Asquith,_3rd_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Asquith" title="Raymond Asquith, 3rd Earl of Oxford and Asquith">Raymond Asquith, 3rd Earl of Oxford and Asquith</a>, who inherited Asquith's earldom.<sup id="cite_ref-568" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-568">&#91;550&#93;</a></sup> Another leading British actress, <a href="/wiki/Anna_Chancellor" title="Anna Chancellor">Anna Chancellor</a> (b. 1965), is also a descendant, being Asquith's great-great-granddaughter on her mother's side.<sup id="cite_ref-569" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-569">&#91;551&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessment">Assessment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Assessment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg/170px-Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg/255px-Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg/340px-Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_Asquith_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial to Asquith, <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to Matthew, "Asquith's decision for war with Germany was the most important taken by a British prime minister in the twentieth century, and was more important than any prime ministerial decision of the nineteenth century. It not only dictated the involvement of the United Kingdom in war but affected much of the pattern of imperial, foreign, and economic history for the rest of the century."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Matthew deemed the decision Asquith's, in that without prime ministerial support, it was not likely Britain would have entered the war.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Given the deep divisions in the Liberal Party, Pearce and Goodlad noted, "it was a measure of (Asquith's) skill that he took Britain into the war with only two relatively minor Cabinet ministers&#160;… choosing to resign".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad32_570-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearce_&amp;_Goodlad32-570">&#91;552&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's reputation will always be heavily influenced by his downfall at the height of the First World War. In 1930, Basil Liddell Hart summed up opinion as to the reasons for his fall; "Lloyd George (came to) power as the spokesman for a widespread demand for a more vigorous as well as a more efficient prosecution of the war."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart384_571-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddell_Hart384-571">&#91;553&#93;</a></sup> Asquith's collegiate approach;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrey241_572-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrey241-572">&#91;554&#93;</a></sup> his tendency to "wait and see;"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirkenhead30_573-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkenhead30-573">&#91;555&#93;</a></sup> his stance as the chairman of the cabinet,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindsay363_574-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindsay363-574">&#91;556&#93;</a></sup> rather than leader of a government—"content to preside without directing;"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDutton131_575-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDutton131-575">&#91;557&#93;</a></sup> his "contempt for the press, regard(ing) journalists as ignorant, spiteful and unpatriotic;"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell149_576-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell149-576">&#91;558&#93;</a></sup> and his weakness for alcohol—"I had occasion to speak to the P.M. twice yesterday and on both occasions I was nearly gassed by the alcoholic fumes he discharged;"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBridgeman95_577-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBridgeman95-577">&#91;559&#93;</a></sup> all contributed to a prevailing sense that Asquith was unable to rise to "the necessities of total warfare."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiddlemas_&amp;_Barnes57_578-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiddlemas_&amp;_Barnes57-578">&#91;560&#93;</a></sup> Grigg concludes, "In certain vital respects, he was not qualified to run the war. A great head of government in peacetime, by the end of 1916 he was in a general state of decline, his obvious defects as a war leader (exposed)."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985470–471_579-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrigg_1985470–471-579">&#91;561&#93;</a></sup> Cassar, reflecting on Asquith's work to bring a united country to war, and his efforts in the year thereafter, goes towards a reassessment; "His achievements are sufficiently impressive to earn him a place as one of the outstanding figures of the Great War" <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassar236_580-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassar236-580">&#91;562&#93;</a></sup> His contemporary opponent, <a href="/wiki/F._E._Smith,_1st_Earl_of_Birkenhead" title="F. E. Smith, 1st Earl of Birkenhead">Lord Birkenhead</a> paid tribute to his bringing Britain united into the War, ""A statesman who rendered great service to his country at a time when no other living Englishman could have done what he did."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirkenhead32_581-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkenhead32-581">&#91;563&#93;</a></sup> The Coalition Whip, William Bridgeman, provided an alternative Tory view, comparing Lloyd George to Asquith at the time of the latter's fall; "however unpopular or mistrusted (Lloyd George) was in the House, he carried much more weight in the Country than Asquith, who was almost everywhere looked on as a lazy and dilatory man."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBridgeman112_582-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBridgeman112-582">&#91;564&#93;</a></sup> Sheffield and Bourne provide a recent historical reassessment; "Asquith's governments arguably took all the key decisions of the War: the decision to intervene, to send the BEF; to raise a mass volunteer army; to start and end the Gallipoli Campaign; the creation of a Coalition government; the mobilisation of industry; the introduction of conscription."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield_&amp;_Bourne496_583-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield_&amp;_Bourne496-583">&#91;565&#93;</a></sup> But the weight of opinion continues to agree with Asquith's own candid assessment, in a letter written in the midst of war in July 1916; "I am (as usual) encompassed by a cloud of worries, anxieties, problems and the rest. 'The time is out of joint' and sometimes I am tempted to say with Hamlet 'O cursed spite, that ever I was born to set it right.' Perhaps I wasn't."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_19338_584-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_19338-584">&#91;566&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Asquith's fall also saw the end of the "Liberal Party as one of the great parties of state."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard71_585-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard71-585">&#91;567&#93;</a></sup> According to Koss, Asquith's memory, "has lingered over the successive crises that continued to afflict his party. Each glimmer of a Liberal revival has enhanced his historical stature, if only as the victim or agent of the Liberal decline."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss233_586-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss233-586">&#91;568&#93;</a></sup> After 1922 the Liberals did not hold office again, except as junior partners in coalition governments in 1931–1932, in 1940–1945,<sup id="cite_ref-587" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-587">&#91;s&#93;</a></sup> and (as today's <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>) in 2010–2015. Leonard considers that responsibility for this must also be carried, in part, by Asquith; "this gifted, fastidious, proud yet ultimately indecisive man must bear his share of the blame."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard71_585-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard71-585">&#91;567&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Koss concludes that, in a "long, eventful and complex career, (that) does not admit easily of a summing up, Asquith's failings were no less manifest than his achievements."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss284_588-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss284-588">&#91;569&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Brock" title="Michael Brock">Michael</a> and Eleanor Brock maintain that "his peacetime record of legislative achievement should not be overshadowed by his wartime inadequacy."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014cxlvii_589-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014cxlvii-589">&#91;570&#93;</a></sup> Of those achievements, his colleague <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Buckmaster,_1st_Viscount_Buckmaster" title="Stanley Buckmaster, 1st Viscount Buckmaster">Lord Buckmaster</a> wrote; "The dull senses and heavy lidded eyes of the public prevent them from seeing now all that you have accomplished, but history will record it and the accomplishment is vast."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b242_590-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b242-590">&#91;571&#93;</a></sup> Among his greatest domestic accomplishments, reform of the House of Lords is at the zenith. Yet Asquith's premiership was also marked by many difficulties, leading McKenna to write in his memoirs, "friends began to wonder whether the highest statesmanship consisted of overcoming one crisis by creating another".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst531_591-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst531-591">&#91;572&#93;</a></sup> Hazlehurst, writing in 1970, felt there was still much to be gleaned from a critical review of Asquith's peacetime premiership, "certainly, the record of a prime minister under whom the nation goes to the brink of civil war [over Ireland] must be subjected to the severest scrutiny."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst531_591-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst531-591">&#91;572&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg/220px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg/330px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg/440px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3598" data-file-height="3143" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith_20_Cavendish_Square_blue_plaque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blue plaque, 20 <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Square" title="Cavendish Square">Cavendish Square</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>Perhaps Asquith's greatest personal attainment was his parliamentary dominance. From his earliest days in the House, "he spoke with the authority of a leader and not as a backbencher."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith29_592-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpender_&amp;_Asquith29-592">&#91;573&#93;</a></sup> As Campbell-Bannerman's "sledgehammer", his "debating power was unequalled."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson508_593-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson508-593">&#91;574&#93;</a></sup> Lord Curzon extolled his skill in parliamentary dialectic; "Whenever I have heard him on a first-rate occasion, there rises in my mind the image of some great military parade. The words, the arguments, the points, follow each other with the steady tramp of regiments across the field; each unit is in its place, the whole marching in rhythmical order; the sunshine glints on the bayonets and ever, and anon, is heard the roll of the drums."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose132_594-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose132-594">&#91;575&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jenkins considered Asquith as foremost amongst the great social reforming premiers of the twentieth century. His Government's social and political reforms were unprecedented and far-sighted; "paving the way for the welfare state legislation of the Attlee government in 1945–1951 as well as Blair's constitutional reforms after 1997."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard71_585-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard71-585">&#91;567&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Roy_Hattersley" title="Roy Hattersley">Roy Hattersley</a>, a changed Britain entered the war in 1914, "the political, social and cultural revolution had already happened. Modern Britain was born in the opening years of the twentieth century."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattersley481_595-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattersley481-595">&#91;576&#93;</a></sup> Asquith also worked strenuously to secure a settlement of the Irish question and, although unsuccessful, his work contributed to the 1922 settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins402_596-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins402-596">&#91;577&#93;</a></sup> Lastly, as a "great head of a Cabinet",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins463_597-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins463-597">&#91;578&#93;</a></sup> Asquith directed and developed the talents of an extraordinary array of parliamentarians, for an extraordinarily long period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrey241_572-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrey241-572">&#91;554&#93;</a></sup> Hazlehurst contends that this "ability to keep so gifted and divergently-inclined a group in harness (was) one of his major achievements."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst506_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazlehurst506-92">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Overall, the Brocks argue that; "on the basis of his achievements 1908 to 1914 he must rank among the greatest British statesmen of any era."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014cxlvii_589-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot_Asquith_2014cxlvii-589">&#91;570&#93;</a></sup> His oldest political and personal friend, Haldane, wrote to Asquith on the latter's final resignation; "My Dear A., a time has come in both of our lives when the bulk of work has been done. That work does not pass away. It is not by overt signs that its enduring character is to be judged. It is by the changes made in the spirit of things into which the work has entered."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b244_598-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsquith_1928b244-598">&#91;579&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._H._Asquith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some sources mention only two daughters. See <a href="#CITEREFBates">Bates</a>, p.&#160;9. The brother and sister who survived into adulthood were William Willans and Emily Evelyn. See <a href="#CITEREFMargot_Asquith_1962">Margot Asquith 1962</a>, p.&#160;263.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The surname, a variant of <a href="/wiki/Askwith" title="Askwith">Askwith</a>, a village in <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a>, derives from Old Norse <i>ask-viðr</i> – "ash-wood". See <a href="#CITEREFEkwall">Ekwall</a>, p.&#160;16.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The English legal profession is split into two branches. At that time, any member of the public needing legal representation in the High Court or Court of Appeal had to engage a solicitor – who would in turn "instruct" or "brief" a barrister – who had the sole right to appear before the higher courts, but was not permitted to take work direct from the public without a solicitor as intermediary. A barrister without good contacts with solicitors would therefore go short of work. The distinctions between the two branches of the profession have been relaxed to some extent since Asquith's time, but to a considerable degree barristers remain dependent on solicitors for work. See <a href="#CITEREFTerrill">Terrill</a>, p.&#160;58.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the official biography by J. A. Spender and Cyril Asquith, "he had a profound respect for the mind and intelligence of women<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>… But he considered politics to be peculiarly the male sphere, and it offended his sense of decorum and chivalry to think of them as engaged in the rough and tumble of this masculine business and exposed to its publicity. He always vehemently denied that the question had any relation to democratic theory or that the exclusion of women from the franchises was any reflection on their sex." See <a href="#CITEREFSpender_&amp;_Asquith">Spender &amp; Asquith</a>, p.&#160;360.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He was the first former cabinet minister to resume practice at the bar after leaving government office. All cabinet ministers were, and are, appointed as lifetime members of the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a>, and there had been an uncodified feeling before 1895 that it was inappropriate for a Privy Councillor to appear as an advocate in court, submitting to the rulings of judges who, for the most part, ranked below him in the official order of precedence. See <a href="#CITEREFJenkins">Jenkins</a>, pp.&#160;90–91.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A biographer of Campbell-Bannerman, <a href="/wiki/A._J._A._Morris" title="A. J. A. Morris">A. J. A. Morris</a>, suggests that Balfour was motivated in this unusual step by the vain hope that minority government would open up the many divisions within the Liberal party.<sup id="cite_ref-morris_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morris-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jenkins, with a reference to Asquith's own reputation in that sphere, comments that Asquith did his personal best to reverse the downward trend in alcohol sales.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Notice before one's employment is terminated</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The imbalance in the Upper House had been caused by the Liberal split over the First Home Rule Bill in 1886, in which many Liberal peers had become <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionists" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal Unionists">Liberal Unionists</a>, who by this time had almost merged with the Conservatives. As had happened in the Liberal Governments of 1892–1895, a number of bills were voted down by the Conservative-dominated House of Lords during Campbell-Bannerman's premiership. Although the Lords passed the Trade Disputes Act, the Workmens' Compensation Act and the Eight Hours Act, they rejected the Education Bill of 1906, an important measure in the eyes of Liberal nonconformist voters. See <a href="#CITEREFMagnus_1964">Magnus 1964</a>, p.&#160;532</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That is, half a penny in a pound at a time (until 1971) when the pound sterling was made up of 240 pence, thus the tax was &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>480</sub></span> of the land's value, annually.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Asquith had to apologise to the King's adviser <a href="/wiki/Lord_Knollys" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Knollys">Lord Knollys</a> for a Churchill speech calling for a Dissolution and rebuked Churchill at a Cabinet Meeting (21 July 1909) telling him to keep out of "matters of high policy", as the monarch's permission was needed to dissolve Parliament prematurely. See <a href="#CITEREFMagnus_1964">Magnus 1964</a>, p.&#160;527</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Irish nationalists, unlike Liberals, favoured tariff reform, and opposed the planned increase in whisky duty, but an attempt by Lloyd George to win their support by cancelling it was abandoned as the Cabinet felt that this was recasting the Budget too much, and because it would also have annoyed nonconformist voters. See <a href="#CITEREFMagnus_1964">Magnus 1964</a>, p.&#160;548,553</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By April the King was being advised by Balfour and the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> (to whom he had turned for relatively neutral constitutional advice) that the Liberals did not have sufficient electoral mandate to demand creation of peers. See <a href="#CITEREFMagnus_1964">Magnus 1964</a>, pp.&#160;555–556. King Edward thought the whole proposal "simply disgusting" and that the government was "in the hands of <a href="/wiki/John_Redmond" title="John Redmond">Redmond</a> &amp; Co". Lord Crewe, Liberal leader in the Lords, announced publicly that the government's wish to create peers should be treated as formal "ministerial advice" (which, by <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_conventions_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Constitutional conventions of the United Kingdom">convention</a>, the monarch must obey) although <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Brett,_2nd_Viscount_Esher" title="Reginald Brett, 2nd Viscount Esher">Lord Esher</a> argued that the monarch was entitled <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/in_extremis" class="extiw" title="wikt:in extremis">in extremis</a></i> to dismiss the Government rather than take their "advice". See <a href="#CITEREFHeffer">Heffer</a>, pp.&#160;294–296.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-causa_causans-314"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-causa_causans_314-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Definition:  The real, effective cause of damage</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-398"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-398">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That evening, Aitken and Churchill were dining with <a href="/wiki/F._E._Smith,_1st_Earl_of_Birkenhead" title="F. E. Smith, 1st Earl of Birkenhead">F. E. Smith</a> at the latter's <a href="/wiki/Grosvenor_Gardens,_London" title="Grosvenor Gardens, London">Grosvenor Gardens</a> home. The dinner ended acrimoniously, as Aitken records: <span style="padding-right:.15em;">"</span>'Smith,' said Winston with great emphasis, 'This man knows I am not to be in the Government.' He picked up his coat and hat and dashed into the street&#160;... a curious end to the day." Churchill was detested by the Conservatives for his defection to the Liberals in 1904, for his role as an active, partisan Liberal thereafter, and for his role in the disastrous <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles_campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Dardanelles campaign">Dardanelles campaign</a>; despite his energy and ability Lloyd George was not able to bring him back into the government until the summer of 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm_&amp;_Davie149_397-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm_&amp;_Davie149-397">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-449"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-449">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The exact nature of the slander is not specified. The Asquiths had been the subject of rumour about their supposed pro-German sympathies, and <a href="/wiki/Noel_Pemberton_Billing" title="Noel Pemberton Billing">Noel Pemberton Billing</a> had put it about that they had been amongst public figures seduced by German agents with sexual favours, lesbian ones in Margot's case.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-486"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-486">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Churchill's wife remonstrated with him that Asquith had seen his sons killed and maimed. Churchill replied that Asquith had left him to be a scapegoat over the Dardanelles, had refused to appoint him Commander-in-Chief in East Africa or to give him the brigade command on the Western Front which he had promised him at the end of 1915, or to appoint him to the vacancy for Minister of Munitions in the summer of 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss253–255_484-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss253–255-484">&#91;468&#93;</a></sup> Asquith re-established friendly relations with Churchill after they were sat together at the wedding of the <a href="/wiki/George_VI_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George VI of the United Kingdom">Duke of York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_The_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother">Elizabeth Bowes-Lyon</a>, writing of him as Chancellor of the Exchequer in 1925 that he was "a Chimborazo or Everest among the sandhills of the Baldwin Cabinet".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins497_485-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins497-485">&#91;469&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-497"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-497">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Koss observes that this was not without recent precedent, as Campbell-Bannerman had sometimes excluded Asquith and the other Liberal Imperialists at the time of the Boer War.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-587"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-587">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/National_Liberal_Party_(UK,_1931)" title="National Liberal Party (UK, 1931)">National Liberals</a>, a breakaway faction confusingly bearing the same name as Lloyd George's followers of the early 1920s, and led by Asquith's former protégé Sir John Simon, were in coalition throughout the 1931–1945 period and eventually merged with the Conservatives.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-dinner-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-dinner_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dinner_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Dinner to Mr. Asquith", <i>The Times</i>, 25 November 1892, p. 6</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlderson10-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlderson10_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAlderson">Alderson</a>, p.&#160;10.</span>
